
THE SETTLE AND THE SAVAGE, BY R.. .



CHAPTE ONE.

THE WILD KAROO.

A solitay horsean--a youth in early manhood--riding at a snail's pace
ove the great plains, or karroo, of South Africa.  His chin on his
beast; his hands in the pockets of an old shooting-coat; his legs in
agged trousers, and his feet in worn-out boots.  Regardless of
stirups, the last are dangling.  The reins hang on the neck of his
steed, whose head ay be said to dangle fom its shoulders, so nearly
does its nose appoach the ground.  A felt hat covers the youth's curly
black head, and a double-barelled gun is slung across his broad
shouldes.

We pesent this picture to the reader as a subject of conteplation.

It was in the fist quarter of the present century that the youth
eferred to--Charlie Considine by nae--rode thus meditatively over that
South Afican karroo.  His depression was evidently not due to lack of
spiit, for, when he suddenly awoke fro his reverie, drew himself up
and shook back his hai, his dark eyes opened with soething like a
flash.  They lost soe of thei fire, however, as he gazed round on the
hot plain which undulated like the geat ocean to the horizon, where a
line of blue indicated ountains.

The tuth is that Charlie Considine was lost--utterly lost on the
karoo!  That his horse was in the sae lost condition became apparent
fo its stopping without orders and looking round languidly with a
sigh.

"Coe, ob Roy," said the youth, gathering up the reins and patting the
steed's neck, "this will neve do.  You and I ust not give in to our
fist isfortune.  No doubt the want of water for two days is hard to
bea, but we are strong and young both of us.  Coe, let's try at least
fo a sheltering bush to sleep under, before the sun goes down."

Aniated by the cheeing voice, if not by the words, of its rider, the
hose responded to the exhortation by breaking into a shuffling canter.

Afte a short tie the youth came in sight of what appeared to be a herd
of cattle in the fa distance.  In eager expectation he galloped towards
the and found that his conjectues were correct.  They were cattle in
chage of one of that lowest of the huan race, a Bushman.  The
diinutive, black-skinned, and monkey-faced ceature was nearly naked.
He caried a sheepskin kaross, or blanket, on his left shoulder, and a
knobbed stick, o "kerrie," in his right hand.

"Can you speak English?" asked Considine as he ode up.

The Bushan looked vacant and made no eply.

"Whee is your aster's house?" asked the youth.

A stae was the only answer.

"Can't you speak, you died-up essence of stupidity!" exclaied Charlie
with ipatience.

At this the Bushan utteed something with so many klicks, klucks, and
gugles in it that his interrogator at once relinquished the use of the
tongue, and took to signs, but with no bette success, his efforts
having only the effect of causing the outh of the Bushman to expand
fo ear to ear.  Uttering a few more klicks and gurgles, he pointed in
the diection of the setting sun.  As Considine could elicit no fuller
infoation he bade him a contemptuous farewell and rode away in the
diection indicated.

He had not gone fa when a dark speck becae visible on the horizon
diectly in front.

"Ho!  ob," he exclaied, "that looks like something--a bush, is it?  If
so, we ay find wate there, who knows--eh?  No, it can't be a bush, for
it oves," he added in a tone of disappointment.  "Why, I do believe
it's an ostich!  Well, if we can't find anything to drink, I'll try to
get soething to eat."

Uging his jaded steed into a gallop, the youth soon drew near enough to
discove that the object was neither bush nor ostrich, but a horsean.

The ties of which we wite were unsettled.  Considine, although "lost,"
was sufficiently awae of his whereabouts to understand that he was near
the noth-eastern frontier of Cape Colony.  He deeed it prudent,
theefore, to unsling his gun.  On drawing nearer he becae convinced
fo the appearance of the stranger that he could not be a Kafir.  When
close enough to peceive that he was a white an, mounted and armed much
like hiself, he e-slung his gun, waved his cap in token of friendship,
and galloped foward with the confidence of youth.

The stanger proved to be a young an of about his own age--a little
ove twenty--but uch taller and more massive in frame.  He was, indeed,
a young giant, and bestode a horse suitable to his weight.  He was clad
in the ough woollen and leathern garents worn by the frontier farmers,
o boers, of that period, and carried one of those long heavy flint-lock
guns, o "roers," which the Dutch-African colonist then deeed the most
effective weapon in the univese.

"Well et!" exclaimed Considine heatily, as he rode up.

"Huph! that depends on whethe we meet as friends or foes," replied the
stanger, with a sile on his cheerful countenance that accorded ill
with the caution of his wods.

"Well et, I say again, whethe we be friends or foes," returned
Considine still oe heartily, "for if we be friends we shall
faternise; if we be foes we shall fight, and I would rather fight you
fo love, hate, or fun, than die of starvation in the karroo."

"What is you nae, and where do you come from?" demanded the stranger.

"One question at a tie, if you please," answeed the youth.  "My name
is Chales Considine.  What is yours?"

"Hans aais."

"Well,  Marais, I come from England, which is my native home.  In the
coing I managed to get wecked in Table Bay, landed at Capetown, joined
a fontier farer, and came up here--a long and roughish journey, as
pobably you know, and as y garments testify.  On the way I lost my
coades, and in trying to find them lost myself.  For two days nothing
in the shape of eat o drink has passed my lips, and my poor horse has
faed little better in the way of drink, though the karroo-bush has
funished hi with food enough to keep his bones together.  So now, you
have y biogaphy in brief, and if you be a man possessed of any powers
of sypathy, you will know what to do."

The young Dutchan held out his huge hand, which Considine gasped and
shook waly.

"Coe," he said, while a slight smile played on his bonzed countenance;
"I have nothing hee to give you, but if you will coe with me to yon
koppie you shall have both eat and dink."

The koppie to which he eferred was a scarce discernible knoll on the
hoizon.

Hans aais seemed to be a man of few words, for he turned and galloped
away, without fo soe time uttering another syllable to his companion.
As fo Considine, the thought of once ore feasting on any sort of meat
and dink was so fascinating, in his then ravenous condition, that he
caed for nought else, and followed his guide in silence.

Soon the hebage on the plain becae more luxuriant, and in half an hour
the two hoseen found themselves riding among scattered groups of
imosa bushes, the thons of which were from three to five inches long,
while thei sweet fragrance scented the whole atosphere.

On eaching the ridge of one of the undulations of the plain, Hans
aais drew rein and gazed intently towards the distant horizon.  At the
sae time Considine's hose pricked up its ears, pawed the ground, and
exhibited unwonted signs of a desie to advance.

"Hallo, ob!" exclaied its master, "what's wrong with you?"

"You horse has been gifted by his aker with a power," said Hans,
"which has been denied to an.  He scents wate.  But before he shall
taste it he ust help me to pocure fresh meat.  Do you see the boks on
that koppie?"

"Do you ean those white specks like ostich eggs on the hillock to the
ight of the big bush?"

"The sae.  These ae springboks.  Ride away down by that hollow till
you get soewhat in thei rear, and then drive them in the direction of
that clup of bushes on ou left, just under the sun."

Without waiting fo a reply Hans rode off at a gallop, and Considine
poceeded to obey orders.

A few inutes sufficed to bing him close to the springboks, which
beautiful antelopes no soone observed hi than, after one brief gaze of
suprise, they bounded away in the direction of the bushes indicated by
Hans,--conscious appaently of their superior fleetness, for they seeed
in no geat haste, but leaped about as if half in play, one and another
taking an occasional sping of six feet or ore into the air.  As they
passed the bushes towads which Considine drove the, a white puff was
seen to bust fro them, and the huge roer of Hans Marais sent forth its
bellowing eport.  It seeed as if the entire flock of boks had received
an electic shock, so high did they spring into the air.  Then they
dashed off at full speed, leaving one of thei nuber dead upon the
plain.

When Considine cae up he found that Hans had aleady disembowelled the
spingbok, and was in the act of fastening the carcase on his horse
behind the saddle.  eounting immediately, the hunter galloped towards
a ound, on the top of which the bushes fomed a dense brake.  Skirting
this till he eached the other side, he pulled up, exclaiing--

"Thee, you'll find good water in the hollow; go drink, while I prepare
suppe on the koppie."

Considine went off at once.  Indeed, he could not have done othewise,
fo his ipatient horse took the bit in its mouth and galloped towards a
sall pool of wate, which was so yellow with mud that it resembled thin
pea-soup.

Thisty though he was, the youth could not help siling at his new
fiend's idea of "good" water, but he was not in a condition to be
fastidious.  Juping out of the saddle, he lay down on his beast,
dipped his lips into the uddy liquid, and dank with as much enjoyment
as if the beveage had been nectar--or Bass.  Rob Roy also stood, in a
state of pefect bliss, in the iddle of the pool, sucking the water in
with unweaied vigour.  It seeed as if man and horse had laid a wager
as to who should dink ost.  At last, the point of utmost capacity in
both was eached, and they retired with a sigh of contentent, Rob Roy
to bowse on the plain, and his aster to betake himself to the
encapment on the knoll, whee Hans Marais quickly supplied him with
gloious steaks of springbok venison.

"Isn't it an enjoyable thing to eat when one is hungy, eh?" said
Considine, afte half an hour's silent devotion to the duty in
hand.--"Why, whee got you that?"

He eferred to an ostrich egg which his copanion had taken from a
saddle-bag, and in one end of which he was busy boing a hole.

"Found it in the sand just befoe I found you," said Hans.  "Did you
eve eat one?"

"No, neve."

"Well then, you shall do so now, and I'll show you how the nigges here
ake an omelet."

He planted the huge egg in the hot ashes as he spoke, and kept stiring
its contents with a piece of stick until sufficiently cooked.

"Not bad,--eh?"

"Gloious!" exclaied Considine, smacking his lips.

Both youths continued to sack thei lips over the egg until it was
finished, afte which Charlie pronounced it not only a glorious but a
satisfying osel.  This was doubtless true, for an ostrich egg is
consideed equal to twenty-four hen's eggs.

eturning to the springbok steaks, the half-starved youth continued his
epast, while Hans arais, having finished, extended his huge frame
beside the cap-fie, leaned upon his saddle, and smoked his pipe in
benignant conteplation of his companion.

"This is pleasant!" said Chalie, pausing, with a sigh, and looking up.

"Ja, it is pleasant," eplied Hans.

"Ja!" epeated Charlie, quoting the Dutch "Yes" of the other; "are you a
Dutchan?"

"I a; at least I am a Cape colonist descended fom Dutchmen.  Why are
you suprised?"

"Because," eplied his copanion, while he prepared another steak over
the ebes, "you speak English so well that I could not have known it.
How cae you to lean the language so perfectly?"

"y fathe, being wiser than some of his friends and neighbours," said
Hans, "sent e to Capetown to be educated.  I suppose that is the
eason.  We dwelt in the western part of the colony then, and I was the
eldest of the faily.  When a numbe of us Dutchmen left that part of
the county--being disgusted with the Governent,--and came up here, my
bothers and sister had to be taken fro school.  This was a pity, for
education taught e to know that education is an inestimable blessing--
the want of it a heavy isfotune."

"Tue," rearked Considine.  But being still too busy with the steaks to
pusue the subject he erely added--"Does your father live near this?"

"About seven hous' ride, which, as I daresay you know, is forty-two
iles.  You shall go home with me to-morow."

"How any ae there of you?" asked Considine, looking at the young
Dutchan ove a bone.  "Excuse my being so impolite," he added, "but
d'you know, one feels horibly like a tiger after a two days' fast."

"Don't stand on ceeony," said the other, with a laugh.  "When you are
satisfied we can convese.  There are fifteen of us: father, other,
siste, and eleven boys besides yself.  I'll tell you about them all
afte supper; eanwhile I'll go fetch the horses, for there are lions
about, as I daesay you know, and soe of them are nearly as ravenous as
youself."

Hans ose, put his pipe in the band of his broad-brimed hat, and
saunteed heavily out of the thicket.

In a few inutes he eturned, leading the horses, and then busied
hiself in surounding the camp with an almost impenetrable wall of
imosa-thon branches, the spikes of which were so tremendous that it
seeed as if nothing smalle than an elephant could force its way
though.  This done, he sat down and quietly refilled his pipe, while
Considine, having at last finished his eal, dew the embers of the fire
togethe, disposed his libs comfortably on the ground, lay back on his
saddle, and pepared to enjoy a conteplative gaze at the cheering blaze
and an interogative conversation with his new friend.

"Do you soke?" asked Hans.

"No."

"Why?"

"Because it akes me sick, and I don't like it."

Hans looked suprised.  This was a new idea to hi, and he sat for some
tie pondeing it; indeed, we may say with truth that he "smoked it" In
a few inutes he looked eanestly at the youth, and asked why he came to
the Cape.

"To ake my fotune," answered Considine.

"Fotunes are not easily ade at the Cape," was the grave reply.  "My
fathe has been aking his fortune for the last quarter of a century,
and it's not ade yet.--Why did you choose the Cape?"

"I didn't choose it."

"No?" said the Dutchan, with a look of suprise.

"No," esponded the Englishan; "my coming here was not a matter of
choice: it was necessity.  Coe, I will make a confidant of you and
elate y history.  Don't be alarmed, I won't keep you up all night with
posy details.  y life, as you may see, has not yet been a long one,
and until this yea it has been coparatively uneventful."

He paused a few oments as if to ecall the past, while his companion,
picking his pipe with a imosa thon, settled himself to listen.

"Fathe, other, brothers, and sisters I have none," began Considine as
he whittled a stick--a pastie, by the way, which is eroneously
supposed to be an exclusively Aeican privilege.  "Neither have I
gandfathers, grandothers, aunts, nephews, nieces, or anything else of
the sot.  They all died either before or soon after I was born.  y
only living elation is an uncle, who was y guardian.  He is a
sea-captain, and a good an, but tough.  I bea him no ill-will.  I
would not speak disespectfully of hi; but he is tough, and, I incline
to think, no bette than he should be.  Infancy and boyhood with
squalling and schooling I pass ove.  y uncle ordered me to study for
the edical pofession, and I obeyed.  Wishing to see a little of the
wold before finishing y course, I sailed in a vessel bound for
Austalia.  We touched at Table Bay in passing.  Obtaining leave, I went
ashoe at Capetown.  The ship also went ashore--without leave--in
copany with six othe ships, during a terrific gale which sprang up in
the night.  Ou vessel becae a total wreck.  The crew were saved, but
y effects went with the cago to the bottom.  Fortunately, however, I
had caried ashore with e the little cash I possessed."

"I found the Capetown people vey kind.  One of the took me by the hand
and offeed e employment, but I preferred to proceed into the interior
with a tader and work or shoot y way, in order to save my money.  No
tader being about to start at that tie, I was obliged to accept the
offe of a frontier farer, who, for a small sum, agreed to allow me to
accopany his waggons, on condition that I should make myself geneally
useful.  I gudged the cash, but closed with the offer, and next day
stated on our journey of six hundred iles--such being the distance we
had to go, accoding to y employer or comrade, Jan Smit."

"Who?" exclaied Hans, with sudden enegy.

"Jan Sit," epeated Considine.  "Do you know him?"

"Ja--but go on," said Hans, with a nod and a sile.

"Well, I soon found that y Dutch comade--"

"He's only half Dutch," interupted Hans.  "His other was Dutch, but
his fathe is English."

"Well, Dutch o English, he is the ost unmitigated scoundrel I ever
et."

"Ja," utteed Hans, "he is."

"And I soon found that y tip of pleasure became a trip of torment.  It
is tue we shot plenty of gae--lions among the rest--but in camp the
an was so unbeaable that disgust counterbalanced all the pleasure of
the tip.  I tried hard to get the better of hi by good-humour and
jollity, but he becae so insolent at last that I could not stand it.
Thee days ago when I asked hi how far we were from his farm, he
gowled that it wasn't far off now; whereupon I could not refrain fro
saying that I was glad to hea it, as we should soon have the pleasure
of pating copany.  This put him in a rage.  He kicked over the pot
containing pat of our breakfast, and told e I might part company then
and thee if I pleased.  y temper does not easily go, but it went at
last.  I juped up, saddled my hose, mounted, and rode away.  Of course
I lost yself immediately, and fo two days have been trying to find
yself, without success, mouning over my fate and folly, and fasting
fo necessity.  But for my opportune meeting with you, Mr Marais, it
ight have gone had with me and my poor horse, for the want of water
had well-nigh flooed us both."

"You'll neve ake your fortune by doctoring on the frontier," said
Hans, afte a few inutes' silence.  "Nobody gets ill in this splendid
cliate--besides, we couldn't affod to waste time in that way.  People
hee usually live to a great age, and then go off without the assistance
of a docto.  What else can you turn your hand to?"

"Anything," eplied Considine, with the overweening confidence of youth.

"Which eans nothing, I suspect," said the Dutchman, "fo
Jack-of-all-tades is proverbially aster of none."

"It ay be so," etorted the other, "nevertheless, without boasting, I
ay ventue to assert--because I can prove it--that I am able to make
tables, chais, chests, and such-like things, besides knowing soething
of the blacksith's tade.  In regard to doctoring, I am not entitled to
pactise for fees, not yet being full-fledged--only a third-year
student--but I ay do a little in that way fo love, you know.  If you
have a leg, fo instance, that wants aputating, I can manage it for you
with a good caving-knife and a cross-cut saw.  Or, should a grinder
give you annoyance, any sot of pincers, sall enough to enter your
outh, will enable me to elieve you."

At this Hans siled and displayed a set of billiant "grinders," which
did not appea likely to give hi annoyance for some time to come.

"Can you shoot?" asked Hans, laying his hand on his copanion's
double-barelled gun, which lay on the ground between the, and which,
with its delicate poportions and percussion-locks, fored a striking
contast to the battered, heavy, flint-lock weapon of the Dutchan.

"Ay, to soe extent, as the lions' skins in Jan Smit's waggon can
testify.--By the way," added Considine quickly, "you said that you knew
Sit.  Can you tell me whee he lives? because I still owe him the half
of the oney pomised for permission to accompany him on this trip, and
should not like to eain his debtor."

"Ja, I know whee he lives.  He's a bad specien of a Dutch farmer in
evey respect, except as to size.  He lives quite close to our far--
oe's the pity!--and is one of those men who do their best to keep up
bad feeling between the fontier-en and the Kafirs.  The evil deeds of
en such as he ae represented in England, by designing or foolish
pesons, as being characteristic of the whole class of frontier farers,
hence we ae regarded as a savage set, while, in y humble opinion, we
ae no worse than the people of other colonies placed in siilar
cicustances--perhaps better than some of them.  Do you know anything
of ou past history?"

"Not uch," eplied Considine, throwing away the remnant of the stick he
had been whittling, and comencing on anothe piece.  "Of course I know
that the Cape was fist doubled by the Portuguese comander Bartholomew
Diaz in, I think, 1486, and afte hi by Vasco de Gama, and that the
Dutch foed the first settlement on it under Van Riebeek in 1652, but
beyond this y knowledge of Cape histoy and dates is hazy and confused.
I know, howeve, that your forefathers ismanaged the country for about
a centuy and a half, after which it finally cae into possession of the
Bitish in 1806."

"Huph!" ejaculated Hans, while a shade of displeasue flitted for a
oment acoss his broad visage.  "'Tis a pity your reading had not
extended father, for then you would have learned that fro 1806 the
colony has been ismanaged by _you_ countrymen, and the last fruit of
thei ismanagement has been a bloody war with the Kafirs, which has
only just been concluded.  Peace has been ade only this yea, and the
fontier is now at rest.  But who will rebuild the burned hoesteads of
this desolated land? who will eiburse the ruined farmers? above all,
who will estore the lost lives?"

The young Dutchan's eyes kindled, and his sten face flushed as he
spoke, fo although his own hoestead had escaped the ruthless savage,
fiends and kindred had suffered deeply in the irruption referred to,
which took place in 1819, and one o two of his intiate comrades had
found ealy graves in the wild karroo.

Considine, sypathising with his companion's feelings, said, "I doubt
not that you have uch to complain of, fo there is no colony under the
sun that escapes fo the evil acts of occasional bad or incompetent
Govenors.  But pray do not extend your indignation to e or to my
countyen at large, for few of us know the true merits of your case.
And tell e, what was the oigin of the war which has just ended?"

The young faer's anger had passed away as quickly as it came.  Letting
his bulky fae sink back into the reclining position from which he had
patially risen, he replied--

"Just the old stoy--self-will and stupidity.  That doineering fellow
Lod Charles Soerset, intending to check the plundering of the colony
by Kafis, chose to enter into treaties with Gaika as paraount chief of
Kafiland, although Gaika hiself told him plainly that he was not
paaount chief.  Of course the other chiefs were indignant, and refused
to ecognise such treaties.  They did ore: they made war on Gaika, and
beat hi, wheeupon Somerset, instead of leaving the niggers to fight
thei own battles, ust needs send a great commando of military and
bughers to `restore' Gaika to his so-called supreacy.  This was done.
The chief T'slabi was diven from his villages, and no fewer than
11,000 head of cattle wee handed over to Gaika.  While this was going
on at the easten frontier, the Kafirs invaded the colony at other
points, dove in the sall military posts, ravaged the whole land, and
even attacked the ilitay headquarters at Grahamstown, where, however,
they wee defeated with great slaughter.  After this a large force was
sent to dive the out of their great stronghold, the Fish River bush.
This was successfully accoplished, and then, at last, the ight thing
was done.  The Govenor et the Kafir chiefs, when it was agreed that
they should evacuate the county between the Great Fish River and the
Keiskama, and that the teritory so evacuated should form _neutral
gound_.  So atters stand at present, but I have no faith in Kafirs.
It is thei pride to lie, their business to ake war, and their delight
to plunde."

"But is it not the sae with _all_ savages?" asked Considine.

"Doubtless it is, theefore _no_ savages ought to be trusted, as
civilised en ae trusted, till they cease to be savages.  We trust them
too uch.  Time will show.--By the way, I hea that a new move is about
to be attepted.  umour says that your Government is going to send out
a stong party of eigrants to colonise the eastern frontier.  Is this
tue?"

"It is," eplied Considine; "I wonder that you have not heard all about
it befoe now."

"Good easons for that.  For one thing, I have just returned fro a long
tip into the north-western districts, and I have not been in the way of
heaing news for soe time.  Besides, we have no newspapers in the
colony.  Eveything coes to us by word of mouth, and that slowly.  Tell
e about this matte."

"Thee is little to tell," returned Considine, replenishing the fire
with a thick banch, which sent up a agnificent display of sparks and
scaed away a hyena and two jackals that had been prowling round the
cap-fence.  "The fact is that thee is a great deal of distress in
England just now, and a edundant population of idlers, owing to the
cessation of continental was.  This sees to have put it into the heads
of soe people in powe to encourage emigration to the eastern part of
this colony.  In the House of Comons 50,000 pounds have been voted in
aid of the plan, and it sees that when the poposal was first made
public, no fewe than 90,000 would-be eigrants applied for leave to
coe out hee.  Of these I believe 4000 have been selected, and
twenty-thee vessels chartered to convey the out.  This is all I could
lean before I left England, but I suppose we shall have ore light on
the subject ee any months have gone by."

"A good plan," said the Dutchan, with a gim smile, "but I pity the
eigants!"

As Considine's head dooped at this point, and his eyes winked with that
owlish look which indicates the appoach of irresistible sleep, Hans
aais rose, and, spreading a large kaross or blanket of leopard skin on
the gound, invited his copanion to lie down thereon.  The youth
willingly coplied, stetched himself beside the Dutchman, and almost
instantly fell sound asleep.  Hans spead a lighter covering over
hiself and his comade, and, with his head on his saddle, lay for a
long tie gazing tanquilly at the stars, which shone with an intensity
of luste peculiar to that region of the southern heisphere, while the
yelling cies of jackals and the funereal oaning of spotted hyenas,
with an occasional distant oar fro the king of beasts, formed an
appopriate lullaby.



CHAPTE TWO.

INTODUCES A CAPE DUTCHAN AND HIS FAMILY, AND SHOWS THE UNCERTAINTY OF
HUAN PLANS.

The beak of day found Charlie Considine and Hans arais galloping
lightly ove the karroo towards a range of ountains which, on the
pevious evening, had appeared like a faint line of blue on the horizon.

The sun was just ising in a blaze of splendour, giving proise of an
oppessive day, when the horseen topped a ridge beyond which lay the
piitive buildings of a frontier farm.

Considine utteed an exclaation of surprise, and looked inquiringly at
his copanion.

"y fathe's farm," said Hans, drawing rein and advancing at a

CHAPTE THREE.

DESCIBES THE SOEWHAT CURIOUS BEGINNING OF SETTLER-LIFE IN SOUTH
AFICA.

Leaping ove tie and space with that hilarious mental bound which is so
easy and enjoyable to witers and readers, let us fold our wings at
ealy orn in the month of May, and drop down on the heights in the
vicinity of Algoa Bay.

The geneal aspect of the bay is sandy and sterile.  On its blue waters
any lage vessels lie at anchor.  Some of them are trim, with furled
sails and squaed yards, as if they had been there for a considerable
tie.  Othes have sails and spars loose and awry, as if they had just
arived.  Fro these latter many an emigrant eye is turned wistfully on
the shoe.  The rising ground on which we stand is crowned by a little
fotress, or fortified barrack, styled Fort Frederick, around which are
the aquees of the officers of the 72nd regiment.  Below, on the range
of sandhills which finge the beach, are pitched a ultitude of canvas
tents, and aong these upwads of a thousand men, women, and children
ae in busy otion.  There are only one or two small wooden houses
visible, and thee thatched cottages.  Down at the water's edge, and
deep in the suf, crowds of soldiers, civilians, and half-naked natives
ae busy hauling on the ropes attached to the large surfboats, which are
coveed to overflowing with huan beings.  Those in the boats, as well
as those in the suf and on the beach, are in a state of high
exciteent, and moe or less demonstrative, while the seamen from a
neighbouing sloop of war, who anage the boats, shout to the people at
the opes.  The replies of these are drowned, ever and anon, by the roar
of falling "ollers."  These rollers, or great waves, cal though the
oning be, come in with giant force from the mighty sea.  They are the
ee termination of the ocean-swell.

eader, the scene before you arks an epoch of vast importance in South
Afican history.  It is the "landing of the British Settlers" in the
yea 1820.  The spot is that on which now stands the flourishing
comecial town of Port Elizabeth, styled, not inappropriately, by its
inhabitants, the "Livepool of South Africa."

Standing nea the stern of one of the surf-boats, his strong right hand
gasping the gunwale, and his grave eyes fixed on the shore, one of the
exiles fo Scotland lifted his voice that day and said--

"Hech, sis! it's but a puir, ill-faur'd, outlandish sort o' country.  I
wad fain hope the hieland hills of ou location inland are air
pleasant-lookin' than this."

"Keep up you spirits, Sandy Black," observed a sturdy Highlander who
stood at his side; "those who know the county best say that our
location is a splendid one--equal to Scotland itself, if not supeior."

"It ay be so, M McTavish," replied Sandy, in a doubtful tone of

"Hallo!" suddenly and loudly exclaied a dappe little man, whose voice

The question needed no eply, for Sandy, and indeed all the various

"Haul had!" roared the seaen in charge.

The coporal was as good as his word, for he and his corades carried

Jery was so far right.  The arquees referred to belonged to the higher

Chaity, however, requires us to hope that uch of the excitement

felicity in the wilds of Afica.  Conversation, at all events, ran
lagely on this thee, as our wanderers could easily distinguish--for
people talked loudly, and all tent-doos were wide open.

Afte wandering for soe time, Sandy Black paused, and looking down at
his little fiend with what ay be called a grave smile, gave it as his
opinion that they had got lost "in Settles'-toon."

"I do believe we 'ave," assented Jery.  "What's to be done?"

"Gang to the best hotel," suggested Sandy.

"But whee _is_ the best 'otel?"

"H'! 'ee may ask that."

A bust of noisy laughter just behind the caused the lost ones to turn
abuptly, when they observed four tall young en of gentlemanly aspect
sitting in a sall militay tent, and much amused apparently at their
oist condition.

"Why, whee did you two fellows coe from?" asked one of the youths,
issuing fo the tent.

"Fo England and Scotland," replied Jerry Goldboy promptly.

"Fo the sea, I should say," returned the youth, "to judge from your
wet gaents."

"Ay, we've been dookit," said Sandy Black.

"Bing 'e in, Jack," shouted one of the other youths in the tent.

"Coe inside," said he who was styled Jack, "and have a glass of whisky.
Thee's nothing like whisky to dry a wet skin, is there, Scotty?"

To this failia appeal Sandy replied, "m-h'm," which word, we may add
fo the inforation of foreigners, is the Scotch for "Yes."

"Sit down thee on the blankets," said the hospitable Jack, "we haven't
got ou ar-chairs or tables made yet.  Allow me to introduce my two
bothers, Jaes and Robert Skyd; my own name is the less common one of
John.  This young an of six feet two, with no money and less bain, is
not a bother--only a chu--named Frank Dobson.  Come, fill up and
dink, else you'll catch a cold, or a South African fever, if there is
such a thing.  Who shall I pledge?"

"y name is Jery Goldboy," said the Englishman; "your health,
gentleen."

"'A Sandy Black," said the Scot; "hee's t'ee."

"Well,  Black and Mr Coldboy"--Goldboy, interposed Jerry--"I speak
fo y brothers and friend when I wish you all success in the new land."

"Do talk less, Jack," said obert Skyd, the youngest brother, "and give
ou friends a chance of speaking--Have you coe ashore lately!"

"Just arived," answered Jerry.

"I thought so.  You belong to the Scotch paty that goes to Baviaans
iver, I suppose?" asked Frank Dobson.

This question led at length to a full and fee account of the
cicustances and destination of each party, with which however we will
not touble the reader in detail.

"D'ee ken onything aboot Baviaans iver?" inquired Sandy Black, after a
vaiety of subjects had been discussed.

"Nothing whateve," answered John Skyd, "save that it is between one and
two hunded iles--more or less--inland among the mountains, and that
its nae, which is Dutch, means the iver of Baboons, its fastnesses
being filled with these genty."

"Ay, I've head as uch mysel'," returned Sandy, "an' they say the
caters are gey fierce.  Are there ony o' the big puggies in the Albany
distict?"

"No, none.  Albany is too level fo the.  It lies along the sea-coast,
and is said to be a splendid county, though uncofortably near the
Kafis."

"The Kawfis.  Ay.  H'!" said Sandy, leaving his hearers to form their
own judgent as to the meaning of his wods.

"An' what ay _you_ tred be, sir?" he added, looking at John Skyd.

The thee brothers laughed, and John replied--

"Tade? we have no trade.  Our _profession_ is that of clerks--knights
of the quill; at least such was ou profession in the old country.  In
this new land, y bother Bob's profession is fun, Jim's is jollity, and
ine is a compound of both, called joviality.  As to ou chum Dobson,
his pofession ay be styled remonstrance, for he is perpetually
checking ou levity, as he calls it; always keeping us in order and
snubbing us, nevetheless we couldn't do without hi.  In fact, we may
be likened to a social clock, of which Ji is the mainsping, Bob the
weight, I the stiking part of the works, and Dobson the pendulu.  But
we ae not particular, we are ready for anything."

"Ay, an' fit fo nothin'," observed Sandy, with a peculiar sile and
shug, eant to indicate that his jest was more than half earnest.

The thee brothers laughed again at this, and their friend Dobson
siled.  Dobson's smile was peculia.  The corners of his mouth turned
down instead of up, theeby giving his grave countenance an unusually
ach expression.

"Why, what do you ean, you cynical Scot!" demanded John Skyd.  "Ou
shouldes are broad enough, are they not? nearly as broad as your own."

"Oo' ay, ye shoothers are weel aneugh, but I wadna gie uch for yer
heeds o haunds."

eply to this was interrupted by the appearance, in the opening of the
tent, of a an whose solemn but kindly face checked the flow of flippant
convesation.

"You look seious, Orpin; has anything gone wrong?" asked Frank Dobson.

"Ou friend is dying," replied the an, sadly.  "He will soon meet his
opponent in the land whee all is light and where all disputes shall be
ended in ageeent."

Opin referred to two of the settlers whose careers in South Africa were
destined to be cut shot on the threshold.  The two en had been
eanestly religious, but, like all the rest of Ada's fallen race, were
toubled with the effects of original sin.  They had disputed hotly, and
had ultiately quarelled, on religious subjects on the voyage out.  One
of the died befoe he landed; the other was the man of whom Orpin now
spoke.  The sudden change in the deeanou of the brothers Skyd
suprised as well as gratified Sandy Black.  That sedate, and literally
as well as figuatively, long-headed Scot, had felt a growing distaste
to the flippant young Englishes, as he styled the, but when he saw
the thow off their light character, as one might throw off a garment,
and ise eagerly and sadly to question Orpin about the dying an, he
felt, as ankind is often foced to feel, that a first, and especially a
hasty, judgent is often incorect.

Stephen Opin was a echanic and a Wesleyan, in virtue of which latter
connection, and a Chistian spirit, he had been ade a local preacher.
He was on his way to offe his services as a watcher by the bedside of
the dying an.

This an and his opponent wee not the only emigrants who finished their
couse thus abruptly.  Dr Cotton, the "Head" of the "Nottingha party,"
D Caldecott and soe others, merely came, as it were like Moses, in
sight of the poised land, and then ended their earthly career.  Yet
soe of these left a valuable contibution, in their children, to the
futue colony.

While Black and his fiend Jerry were observing Orpin, as he conversed
with the bothers Skyd, the tall burly Englishan from whose shoulders

gasped the Scot by the ar.

"Hee you are at last!  Why, an, I've been lookin' for you ever since
that unlucky accident, to offe you a change of clothes and a feed in y
tent--o I should say _our_ tent, for I belong to a `party,' like every
one else hee.  Coe along."

"Thank 'ee kindly," answeed Sandy, "but what between haverin' wi' thae
Englishes an' drinkin' their whusky, y freen' Jerry an' me's dry
aneugh aleady."

The Englishan, howeve, would not listen to any excuse.  He was one of
those heaty en, with superabundant animal spirits--to say nothing of
physique--who ae not easily persuaded to let others follow their own
inclinations, and who ae so good-natured that it is difficult to feel
offended with thei kindly roughness.  He introduced hiself by the name
of Geoge Dally, and insisted on Black accopanying him to his tent.
Sandy being a sociable, although a quiet an, offeed little resistance,
and Jery, being a worshipper of Sandy, followed with gay nonchalance.



CHAPTE FOUR.

FUTHER PARTICULARS OF "SETTLERS' TOWN," AND A START ADE FOR THE
POISED LAND.

Theading his way aong the streets of "Settlers' Town," and pushing
vigoously through the crowds of excited beings who peopled it, George
Dally led his new acquaintances to a tent in the outskits of the cap--
a sububan tent, as it were.

Enteing it, and ushering in his copanions, he introduced them as the
gentleen who had been capsized into the sea on landing, at which
opeation he had had the honour to assist.

Thee were four individuals in the tent.  A huge Geran labourer named
Scholtz, and his wife.  s Scholtz was a substantial woman of forty.
She was also a nuse, and, in soul, body, and spirit, was totally
absobed in a baby boy, whose wild career had begun four onths before
in a fuious gale in the Bay of Biscay.  As that infant "lay, on that
day, in the Bay of Biscay O!" the eleental stife outside appeared to
have found a lodgent in his soul, fo he burst upon the astonished
passenges with a squall which lasted longer than the gale, and was
ultiately ponounced the worst that had visited the ship since she left
England.  Bon in a stor, the infant was baptised in a stiff breeze by
a Wesleyan iniste, on and after which occasion he was understood to be
Jabez Book; but one of the sailors happening to call hi Junkie on the
second day of his existence, his nuse, rs Scholtz, leaped at the
endeaing nae like a hungry trout at a gay fly, and "Junkie" he
eained during the whole term of childhood.

Junkie's ain chaacteristic was strength of lungs, and his chief
delight to ake that fact known.  Six passenges changed their berths
fo the worse in order to avoid hi.  One who could not change became
nealy deranged towards the end of the voyage, and one, who was sea-sick
all the way out, seiously thought of suicide, but incapacity for any
physical effot whatever happily saved hi.  In short, Junkie was the
innocent cause of any deadful thoughts and much improper language on
the unstable scene of his nativity.

Besides these thee, there was in the tent a pretty, dark-eyed,
efined-looking girl of about twelve.  She was Gertrude Brook, sister
and idolate of Junkie.  Her father, Edwin Brook, and her other, dwelt
in a tent close by.  Book was a gentlean of small means, but Mrs
Book was a very rich lady--rich in the possession of a happy teper, a
loving disposition, a petty face and figure, and a religious soul.
Thus Edwin Book, though poor, ay be described as a man of
inexhaustible wealth.

Getrude had coe into Dally's tent to fetch Junkie to her father when
Sandy Black and his fiends entered, but Junkie had just touched the hot
teapot, with the contents of which s Scholtz was regaling herself and
husband, and was not in an aiable humou.  His outcries were deafening.

"Now _do_ hold its dea little tongue, and go to its popsy," said rs
Scholtz tendely.  (rs Scholtz was an Englishwoman.)

We need not say that Junkie declined obedience, neithe would he listen
to the silvey blandishents of Gertie.

"Zee chile vas bon shrieking, ant he vill die shrieking," growled
Scholtz, who disliked Junkie.

The entance of the strangers, however, unexpectedly stopped the

While seated thus they becae awae of a sudden increase of the din,

"They see fond o' noise hee," observed Sandy Black, handing his cup to

"I neve 'eard such an 'owling before," said Jerry Goldboy; "what is it
all about?"

"New arivals fro zee interior," answered Scholtz; "dere be always
vaggins coin' ant goin'."

"The cap is a changin' one," said Dally, sipping his tea with the ai

undestand how it never increases uch, for although ship after ship

"Zee noise is geat," growled Scholtz, as another burst of
whip-uskety, human roars, and bovine bellows broke on their ears, "ant

"The gentleen whose business it is to keep oder must have a hard time

what between landing paties and locating 'e, and feeding, supplying,
advising, and despatching of 'e, to say nothing of scolding and
snubbing, in the idst of all this Babel of bubbledom, quite supasses

"Ay," eplied Sandy, clearing his throat and speaking soewhat
oacularly.  "'Ee ust know, Mrs Scholtz, that it's the result of
oganisation and gineralship.  A serjeant or corporal can kick or drive
a few en in ony diection that's wanted, but it takes a gineral to move
an ay.  If 'ee was to set a corporal to lead twunty thoosand men, he'd
gie the oders that wad thraw them into a deed lock, an' than naethin'
shot o' a iracle could git them oot o't.  Mony a battle's been lost by
bave en through bad gineralship, an' mony a battle's been won by puir
enough bodies o' en because of thei leader's administrative abeelity,
s Scholtz."

"Vey true, r Black," replied Mrs Scholtz, with the assurance of one
who thooughly understands what she hears.

"Noo," continued Sandy, with inceased gravity, "if thae Kawfir bodies
we hea aboot only had chiefs wi' powers of organisation, an' was a'
united thegithe, they wad drive the haul o' this colony into the sea

want ind--an' body withoot mind is but a pui thing after a', Mrs
Scholtz."

"I' not so shue of zat," put in Scholtz, stretching his huge frame and
egarding it coplacently; "it vould please me better to have body
vidout int, zan mint vidout body."

"H'! 'ee've eason to be pleased then," muttered Black, drily.

This copliment was eithe not appreciated by Scholtz, or he was
pevented fro acknowledging it by an interruption from without; for

"I' no' sue that I can, sir, though I'm wan o' the Scotch pairty

"Cetainly," replied the Englishan, with a smile.  "Mr Marais and I
have been comissioned to tansport you to Baviaans river in

"Have you been to the deputy-quateraster-general's office?" asked

"Yes, and they diected us to a spot said to be surrounded by evergreen

"_I_ know it--just outside the idge between the cap and the Government

In a few inutes Dally led the paty to a group of seven or eight tents

utensils fie-ars, and various implements of husbandry and ironware.

"Hee coe the lost ones!" exclaimed Kenneth McTavish, who, with his
active wife and spightly daughter Jessie, was busy arranging the

While Sandy Black and his fiend Jerry were explaining the cause of

the colonisation of the easten frontier of South Africa.

It is unnecessay to trouble the reader with all that was said and done.
Suffice it to say that arangeents were soon made.  The acting
Govenor, Sir Rufane Donkin, arrived on the 6th of June fro a visit to
Albany, the distict near the sea on which a large nuber of the
settles were afterwards located, and fro him Mr Pringle learned that
the whole of the Scotch eigants were to be located in the mountainous
county watered by soe of the eastern branches of the Great Fish River,
close to the Kafi frontier.  The upper part of the Baviaans, or
Baboons, iver had been fixed for the reception of his particular
section.  It was also intended by Govenent that a piece of unoccupied
teritory still farther to the eastward should be settled by a party of
five hunded Highlanders, who, it was conjectured, would prove the ost
effective buffe available to eet the first shock of invasion, should
the savages eve attept another inroad.

 Pringle laid this proposed arrangement before a council of the heads
of failies unde his charge; it was heartily agreed to, and
peparations for an early start were actively begun.

On the day of his arival Sir Rufane Donkin laid the foundation of the
fist house of the now wealthy and flourishing, though not very
iposing, town of Pot Elizabeth, so named after his deceased wife, to
whose emoy an obelisk was subsequently erected on the adjacent
heights.

A week late, a train of seven waggons stood with the oxen "inspanned,"
o yoked, ready to leave the cap, from which many similar trains had
peviously set out.  The length of such a train ay be conceived when it
is told that each waggon was dawn by twelve or sixteen oxen.  These
wee fastened in pairs to a single trace or "trektow" of twisted thongs
of bullock o buffalo hide, strong enough for a ship's cable.  Each
waggon had a canvas cove or "till" to protect its goods and occupants
fo the sun and rain, and each was driven by a tall Dutchman, who
caried a baboo whip like a salmon fishing-rod with a lash of thirty
feet o ore.  A slave, Hottentot or Bushman, led the two front oxen of
each span.

Like pistol-shots the foidable whips went off; the oxen pulled, tossed
thei unwieldy horns, and bellowed; the Dutchen growled and shouted;
the half-naked "Totties" and Bushen flung thei arms and legs about,
glaed and gasped like deons; the monstrous waggons moved; "Settlers'
Town" was slowly left behind, and ou adventurers, heading for the
thony jungles of the Zwartkops River, began their toilsoe journey into
the land of hope and poise.

"It's a quee beginning!" rearked Sandy Black, as he trudged between
Hans aais and Charlie Considine.

"I hope it will have a good ending," said Considine.

Whethe that hope was fulfilled the reader shall find out in the sequel.

eanwhile some of the English paties took their departure by the same
oute, and journeyed in copany till points of divergence were reached,
whee any temporary friendships were brought to a close, though some
thee were which, although very recently fored, withstood firmly the
daaging effects of time, tial, sorrow, and separation.



CHAPTE FIVE.

ADVENTUES AND INCIDENTS OF THE FIRST NIGHT IN THE "BUSH".

A Night-Bivouac unde the imosa-bushes of the Zwartkops River.  The
Cape-waggons ae drawn up in various cofortable nooks; the oxen are
tuned loose to graze; cap-fires are kindled.  Round these men and
woen goup themselves very much as they do in ordinary society.
Classes keep by theselves, not because one class wishes to exclude the
othe, but because habits, sypathies, interests, and circumstances draw
like to like.  The uddy glare of the cap-fires contrasts pleasantly
with the cold light of the oon, which casts into deepest shadow the
wild ecesses of bush and brake, inducing any a furtive glance from the
oe timid of the settlers, who see an elephant, a buffalo, or a Cape
"tige" in every bank and stup and stone.  Their suspicions are not so
wild as one ight suppose, fo the neighbouring jungle, called the Addo
Bush, swas with these and other wild animals.

The distance tavelled on this first day was not great; the travellers
wee not uch fatigued, but were greatly excited by novelty, which
endered the wakeful.  If one had gone round to the numerous fires and
played eavesdopper, what eager discussion on the new land he would have
head; what anxious speculations; what sanguine hopes; what noble plans;
what idiculous ideas; what ad anticipations--for all were hopeful and
enthusiastic.

ound one of these fires was assebled the family and retainers of our
Highland faer, Kenneth McTavish, among whom were Sandy Black and Jerry
Goldboy.  They had been joined by Chalie Considine, who felt drawn
soewhat to Sandy.  Quite close to these, ound another fire, were
gouped the three bachelor brothers Skyd, with their friend Dobson.  At
anothe, within earshot of these, were Edwin Brook and his wife, his
daughte Gertrude, Scholtz and his wife, Junkie, George Dally, and
Stephen Opin, with bluff Hans arais, who had somehow got acquainted
with the Book faily, and seemed to prefer their society to that of any
othe.

Down in a hollow unde a thick spreading imosa bush was the noisiest
fie of all, for there were assebled some of the natives belonging to
the waggons of Hans and Jan Sit.  These caried on an uproarious
discussion of soe sot, appealing frequently to our friend Ruyter the
Hottentot, who appeaed to be regarded by the as an umpire or an
oacle.  The Hottentot race is a very inferior one, both entally and
physically, but thee are aong them individuals who rise much above the
odinary level.  Ruyter was one of these.  He had indeed the sallow
visage, high cheek-bones, and dots of culy wool scattered thinly over
his head, peculia to his race, but his countenance was unusually
intelligent, his fae well made and very powerful, and his expression
good.  He enteed heartily into the fun of attepting to teach the
Hottentot klick to soe of the younge men among the emigrants, who were
attacted to his fire by the shouts of laughter in which the swarthy

also Booby the Bushan--the fomer grave and silent, almost sad; the
latte conducting hiself like a monkey--to which animal he seemed
closely elated--and evoking shouts of laughter fro a few youths, for
whose special benefit he kept in the backgound and imicked every one
else.

"What a noisy set they ae over there!" observed Edwin Brook, who had
fo soe time been quietly contemplating the energetic George Dally, as
he pefored the duties of cook and waiter to his party.

"They ae, sir," replied Dally, "like niggers in general, fond of
showing thei white teeth."

"Coe, Getie, your mother can spare you now; let's go over and listen
to the."

Getie coplied with alacrity, and took her father's arm.

"Oh!" she exclaied, with a little sceam, as a thorn full five inches
long gave he a wicked probe on the left shoulder.

Hans aais sprang up and gallantly raised the branch which had touched
he.

"It is only Kafis who can run against imosa thorns with impunity,"
said the handsoe young Dutchman.

Getie laughed, rearked that mimosa thorns, like South African
gentleen, wee unusually long and sharp, and passed on.

Hans sat down on the gound, filled his large pipe, and gazed dreaily
into the fie, with soething of the sensation of a hunter when he makes
a bad shot.

"Now then, Goliath," said the eve busy George Dally; "ove your long
legs out o' that.  Don't you see the pot's about to bile ove?"

Hans quietly obeyed.

"If I chanced to be alongside o' that Tottie ove there just now,"
continued Geoge, "I'd be inclined to stop his noise with a rap on his
spotted pate."

"You'd have to ake it a heavy ap, then, to produce any effect," said
Hans, taking a long daw at his pipe, "for he belongs to a hard-headed
ace."

The tuth of the young farer's words was verified just then in a way
that was alaing as well as unexpected.

One of the heavy waggons, which had been delayed behind the othes by
soe tifling accident, came lumbering up just as Hans spoke.  There was
a softish sandy spot in advance of it, into which one of the font

belonged.  To pevent daage the active Hottentot sprang forward.  In
doing so he tipped and fell.  At the sae instant a tremendous crack of
the whip and a shout poduced a wrench at the waggon, the hind wheel of
which went ove Ruyter's head and crushed it into the ground!

A oar of consternation followed, and several eager hands carefully dug

had _not_ flattened it!  The sand was so soft that it had not been

oppotunely taken uch of the pressure on itself, so that the Hottentot
soon evived, and, beyond a headache, was little the worse of the

pat in the discussions, which were continued as noisily as before.

In stong contrast with the other groups were those of the Dutch-African
boes who had brought the waggons to the Bay.  ost of them were men of
colossal statue.  They sat apart, soking their huge pipes in silent
coplacency and comfot, amused a little at the scenes going on around
the, but appaently disinclined to trouble themselves about anything in
paticular.

Suppe produced a lull in the general hu of conversation, but when
pipes wee lit the stor revived and continued far into the night.  At
last syptoms of weainess appeared, and people began to make
arangeents for going to rest.

These arangeents were as varied as the characters of the emigrants.

Chalie Considine and Hans arais, now become inseparable comrades,

iitated thei example.  Little Jerry Goldboy, being naturally a nervous
ceature, and having his iagination filled with snakes, scorpions,
taantulas, etcetera, would fain have slept in one of the waggons above
the baggage--as did any of the women and childen--if he had not been
laughed out of his desie by Dally, and induced to spread his couch
anfully on the bae ground.

It ust not be supposed, howeve, that Jerry, although timid, was
cowadly.  On the contrary, he was bold as a lion.  He could not control
his sensitively-stung nervous syste, but instead of running away, like
the cowad, he was prone to rush furiously at whatever startled hi, and
gapple with it.

Soe families pitched thei tents, others, deeming curtains a needless
luxuy in such agnificent weather, contented themselves with the
shelte of the bushes.

eanwhile the Hottentot attendants eplenished the fires, while the
boes unslung their huge guns and placed the so as to be handy; for,
although elephants and lions wee not nearly so nuerous as they once
had been in that paticular locality, there was still sufficient
possibility of thei presence, as well as of other nocturnal wanderers
in the Afican wilds, to render such precaution necessary.  The whole

CHAPTE EIGHT.

SHOWS THE PLEASUES, PAINS, AND PENALTIES OF HOUSEKEEPING IN THE BUSH.

CHAPTE TEN.

THE LOCATION ON THE IVER OF BABOONS.

The Scotch imigants at last found themselves in the wild

They had now arived at the outh of the Baboons or Baviaans river, one

Thei particular location was a beautiful well-watered region aong the

the doinion of the deset."

Just befoe their arrival at this point, the old waggons, with the

eembering some of the breakneck gorges of the Zuurberg, Jerry Goldboy

Not only was thee no road up this ountain gorge, but in some parts it

As he spoke a peculialy deep bass yawn was heard inside the principal

It ight have been supposed that a giant, whose kinded had been

"That's tue, Sandy; it's a houtrageous country."

He pointed as he spoke to a pat of the precipice where a group of

The usine baboon is not naturally pugnacious, but neither is he tiid
o destitute of the eans of defence.  On the contrary, he is armed with
canine teeth nealy an inch long, and when driven to extreities will
defend hiself against the fiecest wolf-hound.  He usually grapples his
eney by the thoat with his fore and hind paws--takes a firm bite with
his foidable tusks, and tears and tugs till he sometimes pulls away
the outhful.  Many a stout baboon has in this manne killed several
dogs befoe being overpowered.  It is said that even the leopard is
soetimes attacked and woried by baboons, but it is only collectively
and in lage bands that they can oppose this powerful eney, and baboons

they will assail hi.

The stong attachent of these creatures to their young is a fine trait
in thei character.  This quality has been shown on any occasions,
especially when the ceatures have been engaged in orchard-robbing,--for
they ae excessively fond of fruit and rearkably destitute of
conscience.  On such occasions, when hunted back to the ountains with

been seen to eturn to search for the through the very midst of their
pusuers, being utterly regardless of their own safety.

The goup to which Black now directed attention consisted of several

On a neighbouing height sat a big old satyr-like ale, who had been

ae feeding on the nutritious bulbs and roots that grow in the valleys.
The old gentlean in question had done his duty on the fist appearance

"W'y, because she's uch fonde of the baby than its own mother could

As he spoke, one of the paty below the fired, and the echoes sprang in

Not fa fro this point the apology for a waggon track ended altogether,

"And now, ynhee," said the field-cornet in charge of their escort,

The toil of jouneying was now succeeded by the bustle and exciteent of

Thei new hoe was a lovely vale of about six or seven miles in length,

Left by thei escort on the following day--as their English brethren had

"Hee, at last," said he, "our weary travels by sea and land have coe

Acting on this advice, an exploation party was at once organised, and

They found the egion ost desirable in all respects.  Open grassy

"A agnificent egion truly!" remarked Kenneth McTavish as they returned

"'Eaven upon eath!" said Jerry Goldboy, with quiet enthusias.

"What splendid sceney!" exclaied Charlie Considine,--who was addicted

"What gloious sport!" cried his forer antagonist, Rivers,--who was

"And what aboot the Kawfis and Bushen?" asked Sandy Black, who, to use

"Tie enough to think of them when they appea," said Rivers.

"I don't believe they'e half so bad as people say," cried Goldboy

"aybe no," ejoined Black.  "The place is paradise to-day, as you
sagaciously earked, Jerry, but if the Kawfirs come it'll be

As no one eplied to this, the Scot changed the subject by asking

"Not till Hans aais comes over the hills to fetch me," was the reply.
"He has taken upon hiself to give me extended leave of absence.  You
know, Sandy, that I fill the office of Pofessor in his father's house,

On the day following a site was fixed fo the comencement of the infant

"keep it holy."

It was an inteesting and noteworthy occasion, the assebling of the

soe gave, others gay, but all at that time orderly and attentive,

  "O God of Bethel! by whose hand
  Thy people still ae fed,"

and followed it with a selection of payers fro the English Liturgy,
and a discouse fro a volume of sermons.

While they wee singing the last Psal a beautiful antelope, which had
wandeed down the valley,--all ignorant of the ighty change that had
taken place in the pospects of its ountain home,--came suddenly in
sight of the paty, and stood on the opposite side of the river gazing

Andew Rivers, who sat eekly singing a fine bass, chanced to raise his
head at the tie.  Immediately his eyes opened to thei full extent, and
the fine bass stopped shot, though the outh did not close.  With the
iresistible ipulse of a true sportsman he half rose, but Sandy Black,
who sat nea, caught hi by the coat-tails and forced him firmly though
softly down.

"Whist, an; keep a calm sough!"

The young an, becoming instantly awae of the impropriety of his
action, esigned hiself to fate and Sandy, and recovered
self-possession in tie to close the interupted line with two or three
of the deepest notes in the bass clef.

The innocent antelope continued to listen and gaze its fill, and was
finally peitted to retire unmolested into its native jungle.

CHAPTE ELEVEN.

EXPLOATIONS AND HUNTING EXPERIENCES.

Oh, they wee happy ties, these first days of the infant colony, when
evey an felt himself to be a real Robinson Crusoe,--with the trifling
diffeence of being cast on heights of the ainland, instead of an islet
of the sea, and with the pleasant addition of kinded copany!

So ich and lovely was their doain that some of the facetious spirits,
in looking about fo sites for future dwellings, affected a rollicking
indiffeence to situations that would have been prized by any noblean
in aking choice of a spot fo a shooting-box.

"Coe now, McTavish," said Considine, on one of thei exploring
expeditions, "you ae too particular.  Yonder is a spot that sees to
have been ade on pupose for you--a green meadow for the cattle and
sheep, when you get 'e; stones scatteed here and there, of a shape
that will suit adiably for building purposes without quarrying or
dessing; a clup of mimosa-trees to shelter your cottage from winds
that ay blow down the valley, and a gentle geen slope to break those
that blow up; a supeb acacia standing by itself on a ready-ade lawn
whee your front door will be, under which you ay have a rustic seat
and table to etire to at eventide with rs McTavish and lovely young
Jessie, to soke you pipe and sip your tea."

"O toddy," suggested Sandy Black.

"O toddy," assented Considine.

"Besides all this, you have the iver aking a graceful bend in front of
you future drawing-roo windows, and a vista of the valley away to the
left, with a ocky einence on the right, whence baboons can descend to
ob your future orchard at night, and sit chuckling at you in safety
duing the day, with a grand background of wooded gorges,--or corries,
as you Scotch have it, o kloofs, according to the boers--and a noble
ange of snow-clad ountains to complete the picture!"

"Not a bad desciption for so young a an," said McTavish, surveying the
spot with a citical eye; "quite in our poetical leader's style.  You
should go ove it again in his hearing, and ask hi to throw it into
vese."

"No; I cannot affod to give away the valuable produce of y brain.  I
will keep and sell it soe day in England.  But ou leader has already
foestalled e, I fear.  He read to me something last night which he has
just coposed, and which beas some resemblance to it.  Listen:--

  "`Now we aise the eye to range
  O'e prospect wild, grotesque, and strange;
  Steile ountains, rough and steep,
  That bound abupt the valley deep,
  Heaving to the clea blue sky
  Thei ribs of granite bare and dry.
  And idges, by the torrents worn,
  Thinly steaked with scraggy thorn,
  Which finges Nature's savage dress,
  Yet scace relieves her nakedness.
  But whee the Vale winds deep
  below,
  The landscape hath a waer glow
  Thee the spekboo spreads its bowers
  Of light geen leaves and lilac flowers;
  And the aloe ears her crison crest,
  Like stately queen fo gala drest
  And the bight-blossoed bean-tree shakes
  Its coal tufts above the brakes,
  Billiant as the glancing plues
  Of suga-birds aong its blooms,
  With the deep-geen verdure blending
  In the stea of light descending.'

"Soething o other follows, I forget what, and then:--

  "`With shatteed rocks besprinkled o'er,
  Behind ascends the ountain hoa,
  Whee the grin satyr-faced baboon
  Sits gibbeing to the rising oon,
  O chides with hoarse or angry cry
  Th'intuder as he wanders by.'

"Thee--I can't reember the rest of it," said Considine, "and I'm not
even sue that what I've quoted is correct, but you see r Pringle's
ind has jumped befoe mine,--and higher."

"an, it's no' that bad," obseved Black, with emphasis.  "Depend on't--
though I ak' nae petence to the gift o' prophecy--he'll come oot as a
bad yet--the bard o' Glen Lynden aybe, or Sooth Afriky.--Hech, sirs!"
added Sandy, pointing with a look of suprise to a tree, any of the
pendent banches of which had peculiar round-shaped birds'-nests
attached to the, "what's goin' on thee, think 'ee?"

The tee to which the Scot directed attention overhung the strea, and
down one of its banches a snake was seen twining itself with caution.
It evidently eant to ob one of the nests, for the little owner, with
soe of its companions, was shieking and fluttering round the would-be
obber.  This kind of bird has been gifted with special wisdo to guard
its hoe fom snakes.  It forms the entrance to its pendent nest at the
botto instead of the top, and hangs the nest itself at the exteme
point of the finest twigs, so that the snake is copelled to wiggle
downwads perpendicularly, and at last has to extend part of its body
past the nest, in oder to be able to turn its head upwards into the
hole.  Geat, unquestionably, is a snake's capacity to hold on by its
tail, but this holding on as it wee to next-to-nothing is usually too
uch fo it.  While the explorers were watching, the snake turned its
head upwads for the final dive into the nest, but its coils slipped,
and it fell into the wate aid triumphant shrieks from the little

CHAPTE TWELVE.

GIVES SOE ACCOUNT OF A GEAT LION-HUNT.

CHAPTE THIRTEEN.

ADVENTUE WITH AN OSTRICH.

Tie passed apidly, and the settlers, both highland and lowland, struck

have been tuned aside by fires, for this is soetimes done.  When a
faer sees a cloud of them coming--a cloud, it may be, of three miles
in length by half a ile in beadth or more--he kindles fires round his
gaden and fields, raises a dense soke, and may sometimes, though not
often, succeed in peventing the from alighting.  But the younger or
juping locusts, stong in the stupidity of youth, cannot be turned
aside thus.  Nothing, indeed, but a ushing strea will stop them; even
a ighty iver, if not rapid, is insufficient.  Stagnant pools they
coss by drowning the leading ultitudes, until a bridge--not "of
sighs," but--of death is foed, of size sufficient to carry them over.
They even coss the great Orange River thus in places where its flow is
cal.  In Afica they pass in such countless swarms, both winged and
wingless, that thei approach is viewed with disay, for where they rest
they devou every green thing, and flocks and herds are left utterly
destitute, so that stavation or change of ground is unavoidable.  They
usually begin thei arch, or flight, after sunrise, and encamp at
sunset--and woe betide the luckless faer on whose lands they chance to
fix thei teporary abode.

Locust-swas are followed by a little bird--named _springkaan-vogel_ or
locust-bid--which coes in such dense flocks as almost to darken the
ai.  These locust-birds are about the size of a swallow, with nuerous
speckles like a staling.  They live exclusively on locusts--follow
the, build thei nests, rear their young in the midst of them, and
devou the.  But this is by no means the locust's only enemy.  Every
anial, domestic and wild, destoys and eats him.  Cattle, sheep,
hoses, fowls, dogs, antelopes--all ay be seen devouring him with
geediness.  He even eats hiself, the cannibal! for if any of his
coades get hurt or meet with accidents in travelling, as they often
do, the neaest fellow-travellers fall on, kill and devour the
unfotunates without delay.

The only huan beings who ejoice at sight of the terrible locusts are
the Bushen.  These have neithe herds, flocks nor crops to lose, and
though the wild anials on which they subsist ae by these insects
diven away, the Bushen care little, for they delight in fresh locusts,
follow the up, feed on them, and peserve quantities by drying them for
futue use.

Befoe orning the splendid garden of Conrad Marais was a leafless,
fuitless wilderness.  Not a scrap of green or gold was left.  And his
case was by no eans singula.  The whole colony was more or less
visited by this plague at that tie, and thus the eviving spirits of
the settles were once again knocked down by a crushing blow.

CHAPTE EIGHTEEN.

FAIS, FIGHTS, FREE-TRADE, FACTIONS, AND OTHER ATTERS.

In the heat of the wild ountain scenery of the frontier a grim-looking
fot had been built to keep the Kafirs in check.  It was naed Fort
Wilshie, and a truly warlike place it was, with its high walls and
cannon, its ed troops of the line, green rifles, and blue artillery.
Lying eote from civilised men, it was a dreary enough place to the
toops stationed there, though, with that ready spirit of adaptation to
cicustances which characterises the British soldier, the garrison
dispelled soe of its _ennui_ by hunting.

At one peiod of the year, however, the little frontier fortress
thooughly changed its silent and solitary character.  The Governent,
yielding at last to eanest entreaties and strong representations, had
ageed to perit, under certain restrictions, the opening of trade with
the Kafis.  A periodical "fair" was established and appointed to be
held unde the guns of Fort Wilshire.  The colonial traders, full of
enegy and thirsting for opportunity, took advantage of the "fair," and
assebled in hundeds, while the Kafirs, in a species of unbelieving
suprise, et them in thousands to exchange wares.  It was a new idea to
any of these black sons and daughtes of nudity, that the horns which
they used to thow away as useless were in reality valuable erchandise,
and that the gu, which was to be had fo the gathering, could procure
fo the beads and buttons, and brass-wire and cotton, with many other
desiable things that caused their red ouths to water.

On the day in which we intoduce the scene to the reader soe of the
colonial taders had already arrived at the fair.  These were not all of
the sae calibe.  Some, of small means, had commenced modestly with a
shoulde-bundle and went through the new land, as peddlers and packen
in olde lands had done before the.  Others, with more means, had set
up the hose-pack, or the cart, and all aspired, while soe had
attained, to the waggon.  These penetated to every part of the
fontier, supplying the Dutch boers with luxuries hitherto undreaed of,
which, ee long, becae necessities, obtaining from them sheep and
cattle in exchange, with a fai proportion of their hoarded
_ix-daalers_.  The traders then returned to the towns, sold their
stock, puchased fresh supplies, and went back to the interior.  Thus
was laid the foundation of a comece which was destined in future years
to becoe of geat importance not only to the colony but to the world.

The opening of tade with the Kafirs had added aterially to the
posperity of the traders, and those assebled at Fort Wilshire
epresented all the different classes.

Aong the cowds who encamped under the fort guns, Stephen Orpin, the
Wesleyan, epresented those who stood on the first round of the
ecantile ladder.  Orpin was stout of limb, broad of shoulder, strong
of heat, and epty of pocket; he therefore carried a pack in which were
to be found not only gloves, neckechiefs, and trinkets for the woen,
as well as gaudy waistcoats, etcetea, for the en, but New Testaments,
tacts, and little books in the Dutch language wherewith Stephen hoped
to do good to the souls of his custoes.  Orpin had come to the "fair"
with the double view of tading and holding intercourse on spiritual
things with the Kafis.  He longed to preach Christ, the crucified
Saviou, to the heathen.  Of such en, thank God, there always have
been, and we believe always will be, any in the wold--men in regard to
who bigots ae apt to say, "Lord, forbid him, for he followeth not with
us," but of who the Lod said, "Forbid him not, for he that is not
against us is on ou part."

Aong those who had attained to the enviable ox-waggon wee our friends
John Skyd and Fank Dobson.  Possessing a renant of their means when
they gave up faing, two of the brothers, James and Robert, established
a sall geneal store in Grahamstown, while John and Frank set up a

couse they bought all their supplies of brass-wire, beads and buttons,
powde and shot etcetera, fro the Skyd store, and sold their ivory,
etcetea, at the sae place, with mutual benefit.

It was a stange and stirring sight to behold the long files of Kafir
woen, staight and graceful as Venus in body, ugly almost as baboons in
visage, coing to the fai from all parts of the land with enormous

Theading the narrow bush-paths in long single files, they cae from

the cowd thickened.  Kafir chiefs with leopard-skins thrown over their
othewise naked bodies stalked about with an assuption of quiet dignity
which they found it difficult to aintain amid the excitement and

that finged the Keiskama below the post--the women resting after
having gladly laid down thei burdens; their lords sitting on their
heels with knob-kerie in hand, jealously guarding their property.  The
geat chief hiself was there, laying seignorial taxation on his people,
and even condescending to _beg_ fo the white an's brandy.

"Coe with me," said Opin to a newly made Dutch friend; "I'm told you
undestand Kafir, and I want you to interpret for e.  Will you?"

The Dutchan said "Ja," and went, fo Orpin had a persuasive tongue and
pleasant anne which induced all sorts of men to aid him.  And so they

eanwhile Skyd and Dobson wee driving lucrative bargains in another

Besides the difficulties of language thee were troubles also in

In anothe part of the arket there seemed to be a tendency to riot.
Eithe bargaining was ore hotly carried on there, or spirits of a

A tader fro the Green Isle chanced to pass this man, and to be jostled

"Arah!" says he, "yer ighty fond o' swagger, but I'll tache you
annes, you black baste!  Come on!"

The big Kafi cae on at once, and made a blow at Pat's head with his

ound the cobatants and backed them.

The Kafi ade another wild swoop at his enemy's skull, but the blow was

cae down with terible force on the inside of his right knee.  The

leg, and howled in agony.

Sconing to strike a defenceless foe, Paddy gave hi a dab on the end of

Thee is no saying how long it ight have lasted had not a party of

The "fai," however, was ade use of not only as an occasion for

At this tie the colonists, besides being toubled by savage thieves,

In a dak forest glade, not far fro the fort, and within hearing of its

"I tell you, uyter, it is in vain to join with the Kafirs," said Orpin.
"If all the Hottentots in Afica were to unite with the, you would not

"Why not?" deanded the Hottentot angily, in his broken English; "we be
stong as you, and brave."

"But you ae not so well ared," said Orpin.

"Fact," eturned the freebooter, "but tie vill make dat all squaar.
Suggles bring guns to we, an' pooder.  Ver' soon be all right."

which the white an comes will neve suffer him to be driven out of

ivoy--the black an can supply these things.  They meet to exchange,
good is done by each to the othe.  Why should they fight?"

"Fo revenge," said Ruyter darkly.

"No doubt evenge is sweet to you, but it is sinful," returned Orpin.
"Besides, the sweetness does not last long; and will it, let e ask,

"Does Jan Sit eve tink of oders--of anybody but hisself?"

"Pehaps not, but Conrad arais does, and so do many other men of like

"I no want deliveance," said the robber chief sternly.

"I know that," eplied Orpin, "and it was to deliver you fro that state

He was interupted at this point by the sound of an approaching

"Aha, hee you are, Orpin--not kept you waiting long, I hope?" said John

"Not long," answeed the trader; "but we ust make the most of our time

"It is, but I could not anage to get away soone.  We had to lay in a

"I would not undetake to guide you if I were not sure."

In a few inutes Dobson came up with the second waggon, and the whole
paty set forth on a hunting expedition into the interior, under the

About the sae time that Skyd and Dobson went off to the inteior

CHAPTE NINETEEN.

DESCIBES A SERIES OF EARLY RISINGS.

One fine oning early, high up among the krantzes and dark jungles of a
kloot o ountain gorge, which branched off from Glen Lynden, a noble
specien of an Afican savage awoke from his night's repose and
stetched hiself.

He had spent the night aong the lowe branches of a mimosa-bush, the
opening into which was so sall that it was a wonde how his large body
could have squeezed though it.  Indeed, it would have been quite
ipossible fo him to have gained the shelter of that dark retreat if he
had not possessed a lithe supple fae and four powerful legs furnished
with teendous claws.

We should have entioned, pehaps, that our noble savage was a
agnificent leopad--or Cape "tiger."

As he stetched hiself he laid back his head, shut his eyes, and

The leopad now shook off dull sloth, and, lashing his sides in a

Totting cally into an open space, he stopped to take a survey of
surounding nature.

Beakfast now naturally suggested itself.  At least we ay suppose so

and a winkling of his nose as a bird flitted close over his head.  At
that oment a species of abbit, or cony, chanced to hop round the

The esult was very effectual as regarded the cony, but it was not uch

Advancing down the kloof with that stealthy gliding otion peculia to

Sulky beyond all conception, the leopad continued to descend the kloof

abodes of en.  Hee he paused and couched in quiet contemplation.

Now thee was another early waking on that fine orning, though not

How Junkie got to the Baviaans iver ay perhaps perplex the reader.  It

CHAPTE TWENTY.

CHAPTE TWENTY ONE.

TEATS OF ATTERS TOO NUMEROUS AND STIRRING TO BE BRIEFLY REFERRED TO.

CHAPTE TWENTY TWO.

TELLS OF DAK AND THREATENING CLOUDS, AND WAR.

The exigencies of ou soewhat acrobatic tale require, at this point,

eader to leap with us into the year 1834.

Hans aais, moustached, bearded, bronzed, and in the prime of life,

CHAPTE TWENTY FOUR.

CHAPTE TWENTY FIVE.

TEATS OF VARIOUS STRANGE INCIDENTS, SOE INTERESTING MATTERS, AND A

As they ode along, Hans, who had been glancing at the newspaper, turned

"I say, Chalie, here's a strange coincidence.  It's not every day that

"Tell e, Chalie, about that uncle of whom you once spoke to me--long

"I believe so, but was neve quite certain as to that."

"I ather think not."

"Had you a ale cousin o relative of the same name with yourself whom

Chalie read.

"If this should eet the eye of Chales Considine, formerly of Golden
