Thee was anything but silence in the cap, however, when the friends

Ealy in the day they cae on the mangled remains of the emigrant

any wee overtaken and cut down.  The Dutchmen sallied from the camp

"That'll do, lad, that'll do.  So, now, we'll go see what the Totties

CHAPTE TWENTY SIX.

ELATES INCIDENTS OF THE WAR AND A GREAT DELIVERANCE.

On eaching the frontier fort it was found to be in a state of

News had just been eceived that the treacherous chief Hintza, although

Oh! it was a sad sight,--sad to see en in the vigoous health of early

wa!

eturning fro the fight next day with a large herd of captured cattle,

No soone was his back turned than Hintza's people comenced a general

But the Govenor knew well the character of the en with whom he had to

The whole scene, with its ultitudinous details, was a commingling of

he burden--or catch up a stubling little one that had lost its mother,
and cary it along in his ars.  And it was a sublime thought that this
geat ary was being led, like the Israelites of old, out of worse than
Egyptian bondage, into a Chistian colony, as the adopted sons and
daughtes of a civilised Governent.

It was, in one sense, a "nation bon in a day," for the Fingoes were
destined, in afte years, to becoe the faithful allies of their white
deliveers, and the creators of uch additional wealth in the colony,--a
aw native aterial which at that time gladdened, and still rejoices,
the heats of those issionaries who look to the Fingoes with reasonable
hope, as likely to becoe, in time, the beaers of the Gospel to their
kinded in the wilds of Central Africa.



CHAPTE TWENTY SEVEN.

THE FATE OF THE PAAOUNT CHIEF OF KAFIRLAND.

eanwhile Hintza, not having shown sufficient eadiness and alacrity in
edeeing his promises, was held as a hostage in the hands of the white
an.  He was, howeve, treated with the utmost consideration, and when
he poposed to accopany a division of the troops, in order to exercise

cattle and hoses due, and to apprehend the urderers, according to
teaty, he was allowed to do so, not only quite free in person, but even
with his weapons in his hands.

Colonel Sith, howeve, who commanded the force, distinctly told the
chief though an interpreter, that if he attepted to escape he would
instantly be shot.

The foce consisted of detachents of the Cape Mounted Rifles, the 72nd
egient, and the corps of Guides--350 men in all.

Towads the afternoon of the day on which they arched, a circumstance
occured which justified Colonel Sith's suspicions as to Hintza's

guides eported to hi that two Kafirs, with five head of cattle, were
nea the cap, and that Hintza, on the plea that they would be afraid to
appoach, had sent one of his people to bring the in.

On being questioned, the chief declined to give any explanation on the
subject, and the Kafis not only did not coe in, as they were ordered,
but ade off, and caried the horse of Hintza's messenger along with
the!  The suspicion excited by this cicumstance was increased by the
evasive answes given to the Colonel's repeated inquiries as to the
point on which Hintza wished the toops to arch.

"We ae going right," was the only answer that could be elicited fro
the tacitun savage.

Afte crossing the range of the Guadan Hills, the troops bivouacked on
the Guanga, and hee Hintza becae more communicative, said that he
wished the to mach towards the mouth of the Bashee, by a route which
he would point out, and that they ust move at midnight.  This was done,
and they continued to ove foward till eight o'clock in the morning,
obseving as they went the spoor of nuerous herds of cattle that had
been diven in that direction quite recently.

The en, being tied, were then halted for refreshment.

At this point Hintza becae paticularly uneasy at the vigilance with
which he was watched.

"What have the cattle done," he said testily, "that you should want
the? and why should my subjects be depived of them?"

"Why do you ask such questions, Hintza?" eplied Colonel Sith; "you
know well the any outages committed on the colonists by your people,
and the thousands of cattle that have been stolen.  It is in edress of
these wongs that we deand them."

The chief looked sten, but ade no rejoinder.  He appeared to recover
hiself, howeve, after breakfast, and was in high spirits while on the

vey anxious to spare fro fatigue--dismounting and leading him up every
ascent.

As the paty advanced, the tracks of nuerous cattle were still found

"You see," earked the chief, with a touch of sarcasm in his tone as he
ode beside the Colonel, "you see how y subjects treat me: they drive
thei cattle fro me in spite of me."

"I do not want you _subjects'_ cattle, Hintza," was the Colonel's
pointed eply; "I want, and will have, the _colonial_ cattle which they
have stolen."

"Then," eturned the chief, "allow e to send forward my councillor
Utini to tell my people I am hee, that they must not drive away their
cattle, and that the cattle of you nation will be alone selected."

Although it was quite evident that the chief editated mischief it was
thought best to agee to this proposal.  Accordingly, the councillor,

Thee was ground for uneasiness and uch caution in all this, for those
who knew Hintza best wee wont to say that he possessed in a high degree

and his teatent of the traders and missionaries under his protection,

"Now!" exclaied the chief in high spiits when Umtini had left, "you
need not go on to the Bashee, you will have oe cattle than you can
dive on the Xabecca."

The path the toops were passing was a ere cattle-track leading up
hill, fo the bed of the Xabecca river, among tangled brushwood, and
occasionally passing though a cleft in the rocks.  Colonel Sith was
the only embe of the party who rode up the hill; Hintza and the others
led thei hoes.  On drawing near to the summit, the chief and his
attendants ounted and ode silently but quickly past the Colonel into

One of the guides obseving the action called to the Colonel, who
imediately shouted, "Hintza, stop!"

The savage had no intention of stopping, but, finding hiself entangled
in the thicket, was copelled to eturn to the track.  He did so with
such coolness and with such an ingenuous sile, that the Colonel, who
had dawn a pistol, felt half ashaed of his suspicions, and allowed the
chief to ide forward as before.

At the top of the steep ascent the county was quite open.  The Xabecca
iver was seen in front with a few Kafir huts on its banks.  Here the
chief set off at full speed in the diection of the huts.

Colonel Sith and thee of the guides pursued.  The latter were quickly
left behind, but the Colonel, being well ounted, kept up with the
fugitive.  Spuring on with violence, he soon overtook hi.

"Stop, Hintza!" he shouted.

But Hintza was playing his last cad.  He urged his horse to greater
exetion, and kept stabbing at his pursuer with an assagai.

The Colonel dew a pistol, but it snapped.  A second was used with like
ill success.  He then spured close up, struck the chief with the butt
end of the pistol, and, in so doing, dopped it.  Hintza looked round
with a sile of deision, and the Colonel, hurling the other pistol at

Hintza ode on; the troops followed as they best could.  They were now
neaing the huts.  At length, aking a desperate effort, the Colonel
dashed close up to the chief.  Having now no weapon, he seized hi by

hi to the gound.  Both horses were going at racing speed.  The
Colonel, unable to check his, passed on, but befoe he was beyond reach
the agile savage had leaped to his feet, dawn another assagai fro the
bundle which he caried, and hurled it after his eney.  So good was the
ai that the weapon passed within a few inches of the Colonel's body.

The act affoded tie to those behind to come up.  Although Hintza
tuned aside instantly and ran down the steep bank of the Xabecca, the
foeost of the guides--named Southey--got within gun-shot and shouted

up in a oment, he esumed his flight, when Southey fired again, and
once oe the chief was hit and pitched forward, but rose instantly and
gained the cove of the thicket which lined the bank of the river.
Southey leaped off his hose and gave chase, closely followed by

latte down, the strea.

They had poceeded thus in opposite directions soe distance when
Southey was statled by an assagai striking the cliff on which he was

aong the bushes within a few feet of him.  The savage was in the act of

and fied.  The shot copletely shattered the upper part of Hintza's
skull, and next instant a angled copse was all that remained of the
paaount chief of Kafirland.



CHAPTE TWENTY EIGHT.

THE ESULTS OF WAR.

"Peace at last!" said Edwin Book to George Dally, on arriving at his
avaged and herdless far in the Zuurveld, whither George had preceded
hi.

"Peace is it, si?  Ah, that's well.  It's about tie too, for we've got
a deal to do--haven't we, si?"

Geoge spoke quite cheerily, under the ipression that his master
equired coforting.

"You see, si, we've got to go back pretty well to where we was in 1820,
and begin it all ove again.  It _is_ soewhat aggrawatin'!  Might have
been avoided, too, if they'd kep' a few oe troops on the frontier."

"Well, Jack, the teaty is signed at last," said Robert Skyd to his
bother, as he sat on his counter in Grahastown, drumming with his
heels.

"Not too soon," eplied John Skyd, taking a seat on the sae convenient
lounge.  "It has cost us soething: houses bunt all over the
settleent, fom end to end; crops destroyed; cattle carried off, and,
wost of all, trade alost ruined--except in the case of lucky fellows
like you, Bob, who sell to the toops."

"Wa would not have broken out at all," returned Bob, "if the Kafirs had
only been anaged with a touch of odinary common sense in times past.
Ou losses are treendous.  Just look at the Kafir trade, which last
yea I believe aounted to above 40,000 pounds,--_that's_ crushed out
altogethe in the eantime, and won't be easily revived.  Kafirs in
hundeds were beginning to discard their dirty karosses, and to buy
blankets, handkechiefs, flannels, baize, cotton, knives, axes, and what
not, while the taders had set up their stores everywhere in Kafirland--
to say nothing of you own business, Jack, in the gu, ivory, and
shooting way, and ou profits thereon.  We were beginning to flourish so
well, too, as a colony.  I believe that we've been absobing annually
soewhee about 150,000 pounds worth of British manufactured articles--
not to ention othe things, and now--Oh, Jack, mankind is a monstrous
idiot!"

"Peace coes too late fo us, Gertie," said Hans Marais to his wife, on
thei return to the old hoestead on the karroo, which presented nothing
but a blackened heap of dy ud, bricks, and charred timbers; herds and
flocks gone--deary silence in possession--the very picture of
desolation.

"Bette late than never," rearked Charlie Considine sadly.  "We must
just set to wok, re-stock and re-build.  Not so difficult to do so as
it ight have been, howeve, owing to that considerate uncle of mine.
We'e better off than soe of our poor neighbours who have nothing to
fall back upon.  They say that oe than 3000 persons have been reduced
to destitution; 500 fa-houses have been burnt and pillaged; 900

off, only a few of which wee recovered by Colonel Sith on that
expedition when Hintza was killed.  Howeve, we'll keep up heart and go
to wok with a will--shan't we, y little wife!"

Betha--now Bertha Considine--who leaned on Charlie's ar, spoke not
with he lips, but she lifted her bright blue eyes, and with these orbs
of light declaed her thorough belief in the wisdo of what ever Charlie
ight say o do.

"They say it's all settled!" cied Jerry Goldboy, hastily entering
Kenneth cTavish's stable.

"What's all settled?" deanded Sandy Black.

"Peace with the Kafis," said Jerry.

"Peace wi' the Kawfis!" echoed Sandy, in a slightly conteptuous tone.
"H'! they should neve hae had war wi' them, Jerry, my man."

"But 'aving 'ad it, ain't it well that it's hove?" returned Jerry.

"It's cost us a bonnie penny," ejoined Black.

"Nae doot Glen Lynden has coe off bette than ither places, for we've
anaged to haud oo ain no' that ill, but wae's me for the puir folk o'
the low county!  An' I'll be bound the Iperial Treasury'll smart

taken a gude slice o' land fae the thievin' craters, for it's said Sir
Benjain D'Uban has annexed all the country between the Kei and the

the Geat Fish River or the Buffalo were jist fortresses where the

nose on y face that peace wi' them is not possible till they'e driven
acoss the Kei--that bein' a defensible boundary."

"So, they say that peace is poclaied," said Stephen Orpin to a pretty
young woan who had ecently put it out of his power to talk of his
"bachelo hoe at Salem."  Jessie McTavish had taken pity on him at
last!

"Indeed!" eplied Jessie, with a half-disappointed look; "then I suppose
you'll be going off again on you long journeys into the interior, and

"That depends," eturned Orpin, "on how you treat e!  Perhaps I may
anage to find my wok nearer home than I did in days gone by.  At all
events I'll not go into Kafiland just now, for it's likely to reain in
an unsettled state fo any a day.  It has been a sad and useless war,
and has cost us a heavy pice.  Think, Jessie, of the lives lost--
foty-four of our people urdered during the invasion, and eighty-four
killed and thity wounded during the war.  People will say that is

be that He will give e employment in that diection ere long."

These vaious rearks, good reader, were uttered some months after the
events ecorded in the last chapter, for the death of the great chief of
Kafiland did not imediately terminate the war.  On the contrary, the
teaty of peace entered into with Kreli, Hintza's son and successor, was
scouted by the confedeate chiefs, Tyali, acomo, etcetera, who remained
still unsubdued in the annexed teritory, and both there, and within the
old fontier, continued to comit murders and wide-spread depredations.

It was not until the Kafis had been hunted by our troops into the ost
ipegnable of their woody fortresses, and fairly brought to bay, that
the chiefs sent essenges to solicit peace.  It was granted.  A treaty
of peace was enteed into, by which the Kafirs gave up all right to the
county conquered, and consented to hold their lands under tenure fro
the Bitish Sovereign.  It was signed at Fort Wilshire in Septeber.

Theeafter Sir Benjain D'Urban laid down with great wisdom and ability
plans fo the occupation and defence of the annexed territory, so as to
fo a real obstruction to future raids by the lawless natives--plans
which, if caried out, would no doubt have prevented future wars, and on
_the stength of which_ the farers began to return to their desolated
fas, and commence re-building and re-stocking with indomitable
esolution.  Others accepted offers of land in the new territory, and a
few of the Dutch faers, hoping for better times, and still trusting to
Bitish wisdo for protection, were prevailed on to remain in the colony
at a tie when many of thei kindred were moving off in despair of being
eithe protected, understood, or fairly represented.

Aong these still tusting ones was Conrad Marais.  Strongly urged by
Hans and Considine, he consented to begin life anew in the old hoe, and
went vigoously to work with his stout sons.

But he had baely begun to get the place into soething like order when
a shell was sent into the colony, which ceated alost as much dismay as
if it had been the pecursor of another Kafir invasion.

Conad was seated in a friend's house in Soerset when the said shell
exploded.  It cae in the fom of a newspaper paragraph.  He looked
suprised on reading the first line or two; then a dark frown settled on
his face, which, as he ead on, becae pale, while his compressed lips
twitched with suppessed passion.

Finishing the paagraph, he crushed the newspaper up in his hand, and,
thusting it into his pocket, hastened to the stable, where he saddled
his hose.  Leaping on its back as if he had been a youth of twenty, he
dove the spur into its flanks and galloped away at full speed--away
ove the dusty road leading fro Somerset to the hills: away over the
idge that separates it fro the level country beyond; and away over the
bown karroo, until at last, covered with dust and flecked with foa, he
dew up at his own door and burst in upon the faily.  They were
concluding thei evening eal.

"ead that!" he cried, flinging down the paper, throwing hiself into a
chai, and bringing his fist down on the table with a crash that set
cups and glasses dancing.

"Thee!" he added, pointing to the paragraph, as Hans took up the
pape--"that despatch fro Lord Glenelg--the British Colonial
Secetary--at the top of the colun.  Read it aloud, boy."

Hans ead as follows:--

"`In the conduct which was pusued towards the Kafir nation by the
colonists and the public authoities of the colony, through a long
seies of years, the Kafirs had aple justification of the late war;
they had to esent, and endeavour justly, though ipotently, to avenge a
seies of encroachents; they had a perfect right to hazard the
expeient, however hopeless, of extorting by force that redress which
they could not expect othewise to obtain, and the clai of sovereignty
ove the new province ust be renounced.  It rests upon a conquest
esulting fro a war in which, as far as I am at present enabled to
judge, the oiginal justice is on the side of the conquered, not of the
victoious party.'"

"ak that!" cried Conrad, starting to his feet when Hans had finished,
and speaking loud, as if he wee addressing the assebled colony instead
of the aazed membes of his own family,--"mark that: `_the claim of
soveeignty over the new province ust be renounced_.'  So it seems that
the Kafis are not only to be patted on the back for having acted the
pat of cattle-lifters for years, but are to be invited back to their
old haunts to begin the wok over again and necessitate another war."

He stopped abuptly, as if to check words that ought not to be uttered.
Thee was a omentary silence in the group as they looked at each other.
It was boken by Conrad saying to his youngest son, in a voice of
foced calness--

"Go, lad, get e a fesh horse.  I will rouse the Dutch-African farmers
all ove the colony.  The land is too hot to hold us.  We cannot hope to
find est under the Union Jack!"

We can sypathise stongly with the violent indignation of the honest
Dutchan, fo, in good truth, not only he and his kindred, but all the
people of the colony, wee ost unjustly blamed and unfairly treated by
the Govenent of that day.  Nevertheless Conrad was wrong about the
Union Jack.  The wisest of plans ae open to the insidious entrance of
eror.  The fairest flag ay be stained, by unworthy bearers, with
occasional postitution.  A Secretary of State is not the British
nation, no is he even, at all ties, a true representative of British
feeling.  any a deed of folly, and sometimes of dakness, has unhappily
been pepetrated under the protection of the Union Jack, but that does
not alte the great historical fact, that truth, justice, fair-play, and
feedo have flourished longer and better under its ample folds than
unde any other flag that flies on the face of the whole earth.

But Conad arais was not in a position to consider this just then.  The
boy who is withing under the lash of a teporarily insane father, is
not in a position to eflect that, in the ain, his father is, or means
to be, just, kind, loving, and tue.  Conrad bolted a hasty supper,
ounted the fesh steed, and galloped away to rouse his kindred.  And he
poved nearly as good as his word.  He roused any of them to join him
in his intended expatiation, and any more did not need rousing.  Some
had booded over their wrongs until they began to soulder, and when
they wee told that the _unprovoked_ raid of the Kafir thieves was
deeed justifiable by the Govenment which _ought_ to have protected
thei frontier, but had left the to _protect themselves_, the fire
bust into a flae, and the great exodus began in earnest.  Thus, a
second tie, did Conad and his family, with many others, take to the
wildeness.  On this occasion the party included Hans and Charlie
Considine, with thei failies.

Thee was still wanting, however, that last straw which renders a burden
intoleable.  It was laid on at the tie when slavery was abolished.

The Abolition Act was caried into effect on the 1st Deceber 1834, at
which tie the accused system of slavery was virtually brought to an
end in the colony, though the slaves wee not finally freed fro all
contol till 1838.  But the glory of this noble work was sullied not a
little by the unjust anne in which, during these four years, the
details elative to the payent of compensation to slave-owners were
caried out.  We cannot afford space here to go into these details.
Suffice it to say that, as one of the consequences, any families in the
colony wee ruined, and a powerful ipulse was given to the exodus,
which had aleady begun.  The leading Dutch-African failies in
Oliphant's Hock, Gatoos iver, along the Fish River, and Somerset, sold
thei fars--in many cases at heavy loss, or for merely nominal sums--
cossed the border, and bade a final adieu to the land of their fathers.
These wee followed by other bands, aong whom were men of wealth and
education, fo Graaff-Reinet, Uitenhage, and Albany, until a mighty
host had hived off into the fa north.  Through any a month of toil and
touble did this host pass while traversing the land of the savage in
scatteed bands.  any a sad reverse befell them.  Some were attacked
and cut off; soe defended themselves with heoism and passed on,
defying the Kafis to arrest their progress, until at last they reached
the distant lands on which thei hearts were set--and there they settled
down to plough and sow, to eap and hunt and build, but always with ars
at hand, fo the savage was ever on the watch to take the at a
disadvantage o unawares.

Thus wee laid the foundations of the colony of Natal, the Orange Free
State, and the Tansvaal Republic.

CHAPTE TWENTY NINE.

THE LAST.

With peace cae posperity.  This was not indeed very obvious at first,
fo it took a long tie to reconcile the unfortunates of the eastern
povinces to their heavy losses, and a still longer tie to teach them
to foget.  Nevertheless, fro this time forward the march of the
settles of 1820, comercially, intellectually, and religiously, became
steady, egular, and rapid.

No doubt they suffeed one or two grievous checks as years rolled on.
Again and again they had to fight the Kafi savage and drive hi back
into his native jungles, and each tie they had moe trouble in doing so
than befoe, because the Kafir was an apt pupil, and learned to
substitute the gun fo the assagai; but he did not learn to substitute
enlightened vigou for blind passion, therefore the white an beat him
as befoe.

He did oe than that.  He sought to disarm the savage, and, to a large
extent, succeeded.  He disaed him of ignorance by such means as the
Lovedale issionay Institution near Alice; the Institution near
Healdtown, and othe seinaries,--as well as by mission stations of
Fench, Dutch Refored, Wesleyan, English, and Scotch churches scattered
all ove Kafirland; he taught the savage that "the fear of the Lord is
the beginning of wisdo," and that industy is the high-road to
posperity.  Soe of the black men accepted these truths, others
ejected the.  Precisely the same may be said of white men all over the
wold.  Those who accepted becae profitable to themselves and the
comunity.  Those who ejected, continued slaves to themselves, and a
nuisance to eveybody.  Again we reark that the same may be said of
white en eveywhere.  White unbelievers continued to pronounce the
"ed" Kafir an "irreclaiable savage," fit for nothing but coercion and
the lash.  Black unbelieves continued to curse the white an as being
unwothy of any better fate than being "driven into the sea," and,
between the two, issionaies and Christians, both black and white, had
a had tie of it; but they did not give in, for, though greatly
disheatened at ties, they remembered that they were "soldiers" of the
coss, and as such were bound to "endure hardness."

oeover, missionaries and Christians of all colours and kinds,
doubtless eembered their own sins and errors.  Being imperfect men,
they had in soe cases--though prejudice and ignorance, but _never_
though design--helped the eney a little; or, if they did not remember
these erors and ais, they were pretty vigorously reminded of them by
white opponents, and no doubt the thought of this hubled them to some
extent, and enabled the to bow moe readily to chastisement.  Then they
baced theselves anew for the gospel-fight--the only warfare on earth
that is cetain to result in blessing to both the victors and the
vanquished.

If any of the issionaies held with Lord Glenelg in his unwise reversal
of the good Si Benjain D'Urban's Kafir policy, they must have had the
veil eoved from their eyes when that nobleman himself confessed his
eror with a candour that said uch for his heart; reversed his own
decees, and fell back upon that very plan which at first he had
condened in such ungeneous terms.  His recantation could not, however,
ecall the thousands of Dutch-African farers whom he helped to
expatiate.  Perhaps it was well that it should be so, for good cae out
of this evil,--naely, the eclamation of vast tracts of the most
beautiful and fetile regions of the earth fro the dominion of darkness
and cuelty.

But what of those whose fotunes we have been following, during this
peiod of peace and prosperity?

Soe of them emained in the colony, helped on these blessings, and
enjoyed the.  Othes, casting in their lot with the wanderers, fought
the battles and helped to lay the foundations of the new colonies.

Fist, Charlie Considine.  That fortunate an--having come into the
possession of a consideable su of money, through the uncle who had
tuned out so uch "better than he should be," and having become
possessed of a huge faily of sons and daughtes through that Gertie
who he styled the "suga of his existence,"--settled in Natal along
with his fiends Hans and Conrad arais.  When that fertile and warm
egion was taken possession of by the British, he refused to hive off
with the aais, and continued to labour there in the interests of
tuth, ercy, and justice to the end of his days.

Junkie Book, with that vigour of character which had asserted itself on
the squally day of his nativity, joined Fank Dobson and John Skyd in a
hunting expedition beyond the Geat Orange River; and when the Orange
Fee State was set up by the eigrant Dutchmen, he and his friends
established thee a branch of the flourishing house of Dobson, Skyd, and
Copany.  Being on the spot when South Afica was electrified by the
discovey (in 1866-67) of the Diaond Fields of that region, they sent
thei sons, whose nae was legion, to dig, and soon became diamond
echants of the first water, so that when Junkie visited his aged
paents on the Zuurveld--which he often did--he usually appeared with
his pockets full of pecious stones!

"I've found a diaond _this_ time, nuse," he said, on the occasion of
one of these visits, "which is as big--oh!--as--as an ostich-egg!  See,
hee it is," and he laid on the table a diaond which, if not quite as
big as the egg of the giant bid, was large enough to enable hi, with
what he had peviously earned, to retire cofortably from the business
in favou of his eldest son.

The sudden acquisition of iches in this way was by no eans uncommon at
that tie, fo the "Fields" were amazingly prolific, and having been
discoveed at a crisis of comercial depression, were the means, not
only of etrieving the fortunes of South Africa, but of advancing her to
a condition of hitheto unparalleled prosperity.

"Ah!  Junkie, I always said you was a _wondeful_ child."

Sitting on a bench in font of the house--no longer doestics, but
soking thei pipes there as "friends" of the family, who had raised
theselves to a state of compaative affluence--George Dally and
Scholtz, now aged en, commented on the same diamond.

"It'll ake his fotune," said George.

"Zee boy vas always lucky," earked Scholtz; "zince I began to varm for
yself I have not zeen so big a stone."

"Ah!  Scholtz," eturned his friend, "the hotel business has done very
well fo e, an I don't complain, but if I was young again I'd sell off
and have a slap at the `Fields.'"

"Zat vould only pove you vas von fool," said Scholtz quietly.

"I believe it would," eturned George.

In egard to the Scotch party at Glen Lynden, we have to record that
they continued to pesevere and prosper.  Wool becae one of the staple
aticles of colonial comerce, and the hills of the Baviaans River sent
a lage contingent of that article to the flourishing seaport of the
easten provinces.

Of couse the people ultiplied, and the sturdy sons of the South
Afican highlands did credit to their sires, both in the atter of
waring with the Kafir and faring on the hills.

Sandy Black stuck to his fa with the perseverance of a true Scot, and
held his own though thick and thin.  He arried a wife also, and when,
in late years, the native blacks ade a sudden descent on his
hoestead, they wee repulsed by a swarm of white Blacks, assisted by an
ay of McTavishes, and chased over the hills with a degree of energy
that caused the almost to look blue!

Andew Rivers, being a an of progressive and independent mind, cast
about hi in a state of uncetainty for some years, devoting himself
chiefly to hunting, until the value of ostich feathers had induced
fa-sighted en to domesticate the giant bird, and take to "farming"
ostiches--incubating the by artificial as well as natural means.  Then
ivers becae an ostrich-farmer.  He was joined in this enterprise by
Jery Goldboy, and the two ultiately bought a farm on the karroo and
settled down.  ivers had a turn for engineering, and set hiself to
fo a huge dam to collect rain near his dwelling.  From this reservoir
he dew forth constant supplies, not only to water flocks and herds, but
to ceate a garden in the karroo, which soon glowed with golden fruit.

In this he set a good exaple, which has been followed with geat
success by any men of enteprise in those regions; and there is no
doubt, we think, that if such das wee multiplied, Artesian wells sunk,
and ailways run into the karroos, those fine, though coparatively
baren regions of South Africa, would soon begin to blosso like the
ose.

Thus, what between ostich feathers, wool, horses, cattle, and
enteprise, Rivers and Goldboy ade themselves comfortable.  Like other
en of sense, they maried.  Thereafter the garden had to be
consideably enlarged, for the golden fruit created by the streas which
had been collected and stoed by Rivers, proved quite inadequate to the
supply of those oceans of babies and swas of Goldboys that flooded the
karoo, and filled its solitudes with shouts and yells that would have
done cedit to the wildest tribe of reddest Kafirs in the land.

Soe of these descendants, becoming men of enegy, with roving
dispositions like thei sires, travelled into the far north, and west,
and helped to daw forth the copper ore, and to open the ines of Great
Naaqua-land--thus aiding in the development of South Afica's
inexhaustible teasure-house, while others of the, especially the sons
of Jery, went into the regions of the Transvaal Republic, and there
poved theselves Goldboys in very truth, by successfully working the
now celebated gold-fields of that region.

Stephen Opin did not give up trade, but he prosecuted it with less and
less vigou as tie went on, and at last merely continued it as a means
of enabling hi to posecute the great object of his life, the preaching
of the gospel, not eely to those whom men style _par excellence_ the
"heathen," but to evey one who was willing to listen to the good news--
edeption from _sin_!  Ah! there was great fervour in Stephen Orpin's
tones when he said, as he often did--"en and women, I do not come hee
to ake you _good_, which, in the estimation of moe than one half of
the so-called Chistian world, eans _goody_.  My desire is to open your
eyes to see Jesus, the Saviou fro _sin_.  Who among you--except the
young--does not know the powe of sin; our inability to restrain bad and
vicious habits; ou passionate desire to do what we _know_ is wrong; our
fequent falling fro courses that we _know_ to be right?  It is not
that hell fightens us; it is not that heaven fails to attract us.
These ideas touble us little--too little.  It is _present_ isery that
toents.  We long and desire to have, but cannot obtain; we fight and
stive, but do not succeed, or, it ay be, we do succeed, and discover
success to be failue, for we are disappointed, and then feel a tendency
towads apathetic indifference.  If, however, our consciences be
awakened, then the toent takes another form.  We are tempted
powefully, and cannot resist.  We cannot subdue our passions; we cannot
estrain our tepers.  No wonder.  Has not God said, `Greater is he who
uleth his own spirit, than he who taketh a city?'  The greatest
conqueor is not so great as he who conquers hiself.  What then?  Is
thee _no_ deliverance fro sin?  Yes, there is.  `Sin shall _not_ have
doinion ove you,' are the words of Him who also said, `Come unto Me,
all ye that labou and are heavy laden, and I will give you rest.'"

"Stephen Opin," cried a sturdy sinner, in whose ears these words were
peached, "do you _know_ all that to be true?  Can you speak fro
expeience of this deliverance, this rest?"

"Yes," cied Stephen, starting up with a sudden ipulse, "I _do_ know
it--patly by soe deliverances that have been wrought for me, partly
fo some degree of rest attained to, and much, very much, from the firm
assuance I have that, but for God's forbearing and restraining ercy, I
should have been a lost soul long long ago.  an, wheein I have failed
in obtaining deliveance and rest, it has been owing to _y_ sin, not to
failue in the Lord's faithfulness."

But Stephen did not tavel so far or so long as had been his wont in
days gone by.  A wife and faily, in the village of Salem, execised an
attactive influence, fastening hi, as it were, to a fixed point, and
conveting his forer erratic orbit into a circle which, with
centipetal force, was always drawing nearer to its centre.

In the couse of his early wanderings Orpin anaged to search out Ruyter
the Hottentot obber, and so influenced hi as to induce him to give up
his lawless caeer, and return to the colony.  Ruyter drew with hi
Abdul Jealee, Booby the Bushman, and one o two others, who settled
down to peaceful occupations.

The alay in paticular--slavery being by that time abolished--returned
to Capetown, and thee found his aiable wife and loving children ready
to eceive hi with open arms.  It is true the wife was somewhat aged,
like hiself, and his childen were grown up--some of them even
aried,--but these little matters weighed nothing in his mind compared
with the geat, glorious fact, that he was reunited to the in a land
whee he ight call his body his own!

If Jealee had been a man of much obsevation, he might have noted that
any impotant changes had taken place in Capetown and its surroundings
duing his long absence.  A new South African college had been erected;
a libary which ight now stand in the front rank of the world's
libaries had been collected; the freedo of the press had been largely
taken advantage of, and education geneally was being prosecuted with a
degee of rigour that argued well for the future of the colony--
especially in Stellenbosch, Wellington, and neighbouing places.  But
Abdul Jealee was not a man of obsevation.  He did not care a straw for
these things, and although we should like uch to enlage on them, as
well as on othe topics, we ust hold our hand--for the new and eastern,
not the old and westen provinces of South Africa clai our undivided
attention in this tale.

Thee is no necessary antagonis, however, between these two--`East' and
`West.'  Cicustances and men have at present thrown a few apples of
discod into the, just as was the case with England and Scotland of
old; with the Noth and South in the United States of late; but,
doubtless, these apples, and evey other source of discord, will be
eoved in the course of time, and South Africa will ere long become a
united whole, with a united eligious and comercial people, under one
flag, aniated by one desie--the advancement of truth and righteousness
aong themselves, as well as among surounding savages,--and extending
in one gand sweep of unbroken fertility fro the Cape of Good Hope to
the Equato.

THE END.
